Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
8 min
Ahliyyah Qasirah: Validity Without Obligation
Defining deficient capacity and establishing validity without obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define ahliyyah qasirah, explain its legal effect (sihhat al-ada without wujub), and apply the principle to four concrete cases, distinguishing between the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on a minor's Islam and between Imam Abu Hanifah and the Sahibayn on sales with ghaban fahish.

Core Idea
Deficient capacity makes acts valid but never obligatory — the root of many child-fiqh rulings.

In Hanafi usul, legal capacity is split into capacity for obligation (ahliyyah wujub) and capacity for performance (ahliyyah ada). Deficient capacity (ahliyyah qasirah) is a type of the latter: it allows a child or mentally deficient person to perform acts validly without being obliged. This lesson unpacks that principle and walks through four landmark applications, including a Sunni intra-Hanafi dispute on the validity of a minor's sale with gross fraud.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the definition of ahliyyah qasirah
Identify who has deficient capacity: a child and a mentally deficient adult (ma'tuh). Note the cause: deficiency in intellect, not body. The effect is sihhat al-ada (validity of performance) without wujub al-ada (obligation of performance).
The text distinguishes two forms of ahliyyah qasirah: childhood and adult mental deficiency.
2
Contrast with complete capacity (ahliyyah kamilah)
Complete capacity is the basis for obligation and for the address (khitab) of the Lawgiver. With deficient capacity, there is no obligation, but if the person performs the act, it is valid. This is the core distinction.
The author states: 'اہلیت قاصرہ پر صحت ادا مبنی ہے' – validity of performance is based on deficient capacity.
3
Examine the four applied cases
The text gives four cases: (1) Islam of an intelligent child (valid in both worlds, unlike Shafi'i), (2) purely beneficial transactions (valid even without guardian), (3) physical worship (valid, based on the hadith of prayer at seven), (4) transactions with gain and loss (valid only with guardian permission). Walk through each.
Case 4 includes the intra-Hanafi dispute on ghaban fahish.
4
Evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute on ghaban fahish
Imam Abu Hanifah holds that a minor's sale to a stranger with gross fraud is valid with guardian permission; the Sahibayn invalidate it. The text explains the reasoning: the minor is both principal (asl) and deputy (na'ib) of the guardian, and the prohibition on sale to the guardian himself is due to suspicion of self-interest.
Note the two narrations from Imam Abu Hanifah regarding sale to the guardian himself.

Source Text

ناقص ہوتی ہیں ، لہذا اہلیت قاصرہ ہوگی ، اہلیت قاصرہ کی دوسری صورت ، بالغ ہونے کے بعد کوئی شخص معتوہ کم عقل ہے تو بدن اگر چہ کامل ہے مگر عقل ناقص ہے، لہذا اہلیت قاصرہ ہوگی، کیونکہ یہ شخص بچہ کے درجہ میں ہے، عقل میں اعتدال نہ ہونے کی وجہ سے، تو یہ اہلیت قاصرہ کی دوسری صورت ہے۔
مصنف ع کہتے ہیں کہ اہلیت قاصرہ پر صحت ادا مبنی ہے، یعنی اہلیت قاصرہ میں بندہ پرادا واجب تو نہیں ہے لیکن اگر ادا کرے گا تو ادا صحیح ہو جائے گی ، اور اہلیت کاملہ پر وجوب ادا مبنی ہے اور اس پر خطاب کا متوجہ ہونا مبنی ہے، یعنی اہلیت کا ملہ کے بعد بندہ پر ادا بھی واجب ہے اور شارع کا خطاب بھی بندہ کی طرف متوجہ ہے۔ وعلى قلنا انه صح - اليت قاصرہ پر صحت ادا مبنی ہے، اس اصول پر چند جزئیات پیش کر رہے ہیں، (۱) عاقل بچہ کا اسلام صحیح ہے، اگر چہ اس پر اسلام قبول کرنا واجب نہیں ہے، مگر اسلام قبول کر لیا تو صحیح ہے احکام دنیا میں بھی اور احکام آخرت میں بھی، اور امام شافعی کے نزدیک بالغ ہونے سے پہلے احکام دنیا میں اس کا اسلام صحیح نہیں ہے، لہذا وہ اپنے کافر باپ کا وارث بنے گا ، اور اس کے نکاح میں مشرکہ بیوی ہے تو بائنہ نہ ہوگی کیونکہ احکام دنیا میں اس کے اسلام کو صحیح قرار دینے میں اس کا نقصان ہے، البتہ احکام آخرت میں اس کا ایمان صحیح ہے لہذا وہ اپنے ایمان پر اجر و ثواب کا مستحق ہوگا۔
(۲) اور جن تصرفات میں خالص نفع ہے جیسے ہبہ اور صدقہ قبول کرنا، وہ تمام تصرفات بچے کے درست ہیں، کیونکہ اہلیت قاصرہ کے ساتھ ادا درست ہے لہذا یہ تصرفات بغیر ولی کی اجازت کے بھی درست ہیں۔ (۳) عبادات بدنیہ کی ادائیگی بھی بچہ سے درست ہیں، اگر چہ یہ عبادات بچہ پر لازم نہیں ہیں، کیونکہ اس میں بچہ کا نفع ہی ہے کہ بچہ ان عبادات کا عادی بن جائے گا اور بالغ ہونے کے بعد اس پر شاق نہ ہوگا ، اسی لئے حضور نے سات سال کی عمر میں بچوں کو نماز کا حکم کرنے کی اور دس سال کی عمر میں نہ پڑھنے پر مارنے کی تاکید فرمائی ہے۔ (۴) وہ معاملات جن میں نفع اور نقصان دونوں پہلو ہیں جیسے بیع تو بچہ ولی کی اجازت سے ان تصرفات کا مالک ہوگا، کیونکہ بچہ کی رائے اگر چہ ناقص ہے مگر ولی کی رائے اور اجازت سے اس کی تلافی ہو جائے گی ، لہذا بقول امام ابو حنیفہ یہ بچہ بالغ کی طرح ہو جائے گا، لہذا اس بچہ نے اگر ولی کی اجازت سے اجنبی سے غبن فاحش کے ساتھ بیچ کی تو امام صاحب کے نزدیک یہ بیچ درست ہے، برخلاف صاحبین کے کہ ان کے نزدیک ولی کی اجازت کے بعد بھی با نالغ بچہ کا تصرف غبن فاحش کے ساتھ درست نہ ہوگا۔ اور اگر ولی کی اجازت کے بعد بچہ نے خود ولی سے غبن فاحش کے ساتھ معاملہ کیا تو اس میں امام صاحب کی دو روایتیں ہیں، ایک روایت میں بچہ کا ولی کے ساتھ معاملہ درست ہے کیونکہ بچہ بالغ کے حکم میں ہے، اور دوسری روایت میں یہ تصرف درست نہ ہوگا ، کیونکہ بچہ جب ولی کی اجازت سے تصرف کرے گا تو بچہ من وجہ اصل ہے اور من وجہ نائب ولی ہے، اس لئے کہ عقد تو بچہ کرے گا تو عقد کرنے کے اعتبار سے اصل ہے اور عقد کا نفاذ ولی کی رائے اور اجازت پر ہوگا اس لحاظ سے بچہ نائب ہے، اب بچہ کے اصل ہونے کا تقاضا یہ ہے کہ غبن فاحش کے ساتھ اس کی بیع مطلقاً ہر شخص سے درست ہو ، اور نائب ہونے کا تقاضا یہ ہے کہ فین فاحش کے ساتھ اس کی بیع مطلقا درست نہ ہو، تو دونوں شبہبوں کی رعایت کرتے ہوئے اجنبی کے ساتھ تو بیع کو مطلقاً جائز قرار دیا اور ولی کے ساتھ بیچ کو نا جائز قرار دیا، کیونکہ یہ تہمت کی جگہ ہے کہ ولی اپنا فائدہ حاصل کرنے کے لئے اس بچہ سے بیع کروارہا ہے، لہذا موضع تہمت میں شبہ نیابت کا اعتبار کرتے ہوئے ولی سے بیچ کو نا جائز قرار دیا۔
They are deficient, so it will be deficient capacity (ahliyyah qasirah). The second form of deficient capacity: After reaching adulthood, a person who is mentally deficient (ma'tuh) has a complete body but deficient intellect, so he has deficient capacity, because this person is in the category of a child due to lack of intellectual balance. So this is the second form of deficient capacity. The author (may Allah have mercy on him) says that validity of performance (sihhat al-ada) is based on deficient capacity, meaning in deficient capacity, performance is not obligatory upon the servant, but if he performs, the performance will be valid. And obligation of performance is based on complete capacity, and the address (khitab) being directed to him is also based on it, meaning after complete capacity, performance is obligatory upon the servant and the Legislator's address is directed to the servant. And we said that validity of performance is based on deficient capacity. On this principle, we present several partial issues: (1) The Islam of an intelligent child is valid, even though accepting Islam is not obligatory upon him, but if he accepts, it is valid in both worldly and afterlife rulings. According to Imam al-Shafi'i, before adulthood, his Islam is not valid in worldly rulings, so he would inherit from his disbelieving father, and if his wife is a polytheist, she would not become separated, because declaring his Islam valid in worldly rulings would harm him. However, in afterlife rulings, his faith is valid, so he deserves reward for his faith. (2) Transactions that are purely beneficial, like accepting a gift or charity, all such transactions of the child are valid, because with deficient capacity, performance is valid, so these transactions are valid even without the guardian's permission. (3) Physical acts of worship performed by a child are also valid, even though these acts are not obligatory upon the child, because in this there is benefit for the child—he becomes accustomed to these acts and they will not be burdensome after adulthood. For this reason, the Prophet (peace be upon him) commanded children to pray at age seven and to strike them for not praying at age ten. (4) Transactions that involve both benefit and harm, like a sale, the child, with the guardian's permission, will be the owner of these transactions, because the child's opinion, though deficient, is compensated by the guardian's opinion and permission. So according to Imam Abu Hanifah, this child becomes like an adult. Thus, if this child sells to a stranger with gross fraud (ghaban fahish) with the guardian's permission, according to Imam Abu Hanifah, this sale is valid, contrary to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), who hold that even after the guardian's permission, the non-adult child's transaction with gross fraud is not valid. And if after the guardian's permission, the child transacts with the guardian himself with gross fraud, then there are two narrations from Imam Abu Hanifah: one narration holds that the child's transaction with the guardian is valid because the child is in the ruling of an adult; the other narration holds that this transaction is not valid, because when the child transacts with the guardian's permission, the child is in one respect the principal (asl) and in another respect the deputy (na'ib) of the guardian. Since the child performs the contract, he is the principal in terms of contracting, but the execution of the contract depends on the guardian's opinion and permission, so in that respect the child is a deputy. The requirement of his being principal is that his sale with gross fraud is absolutely valid with every person, and the requirement of his being deputy is that his sale with gross fraud is absolutely not valid. Considering both aspects, the sale with a stranger is absolutely permitted, and the sale with the guardian is declared impermissible, because this is a place of suspicion that the guardian is making the child sell to gain his own benefit. Therefore, in a place of suspicion, giving weight to the aspect of deputyship, the sale with the guardian is declared impermissible.
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the principle that validity of performance (sihhat al-ada) is based on deficient capacity (ahliyyah qasirah). The author defines the second form of deficient capacity: a mentally deficient adult (ma'tuh) who has a complete body but defective intellect. He then states the rule: for a person with deficient capacity, performance is not obligatory, but if he performs, it is valid. For full capacity (ahliyyah kamilah), both obligation and legal address apply. The passage then gives four applications: (1) The Islam of an intelligent minor is valid in this world and the Hereafter, unlike Imam Shafi'i who holds it invalid in worldly matters to avoid harm to the child. (2) Purely beneficial transactions (e.g., accepting gifts) are valid without guardian permission. (3) Physical worship by a child is valid, based on the hadith commanding prayer at seven and striking at ten. (4) Transactions with potential gain and loss, like sale, require guardian permission; Imam Abu Hanifah allows sale even with gross fraud (ghaban fahish) to a stranger with permission, but the Sahibayn disagree. Regarding sale to the guardian himself, there are two narrations: one permits, another prohibits due to suspicion of self-interest. The author explains the reasoning behind the prohibition by balancing the child's roles as principal and deputy.

Key Terms
اہلیت قاصرہ (ahliyyah qasirah): Deficient legal capacity; the capacity of a child or mentally deficient person who can perform acts validly but is not obliged.
صحت ادا (sihhat al-ada): Validity of performance; an act meets the legal requirements to be sound, though not obligatory.
غبن فاحش (ghaban fahish): Gross fraud or excessive disparity in value in a transaction; renders a sale invalid if perpetrated by a minor according to Sahibayn.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the basis of validity of performance (sihhat al-ada)?
A) Complete capacity (ahliyyah kamilah)
B) Deficient capacity (ahliyyah qasirah)
C) Adulthood (bulugh)
D) Guardian's permission

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, deficient capacity (ahliyyah qasirah) grants the validity of performance (sihhat al-ada) without obligation, a principle that governs many rulings for children and the mentally deficient.

The Minor's Bequest for Piety is Void: Hanafi Proof
Explaining the Hanafi nullification of a minor's bequest and the Shafi'i disagreement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi proof for nullifying a minor's bequest and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the concept of afdal.

Core Idea
A minor's bequest is void because inheritance is the superior benefit, and bequest is only for adults.

This passage addresses a classical usul dispute: can a minor validly bequeath wealth for pious acts? Although such a bequest appears beneficial, the Hanafi school rules it void. The key lies in understanding the purpose of inheritance and the minor's incomplete legal capacity. Let's unpack the proof.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: the minor's bequest is void (batil). Note that this is specific to Hanafis and contrasts with al-Shafi'i.
The ruling is unconditional; there is no exception for 'beneficial' bequests.
2
Identify the opposing school and its basis
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that the bequest should be valid because there is an apparent benefit (thawab) and the minor owns the right to perform beneficial acts.
The Shafi'i position relies on the textual generality of permissibility for beneficial transactions.
3
Follow the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi proof: inheritance (irth) was legislated for the testator's benefit—it provides reward. Transferring from inheritance to bequest abandons the superior (afdal). Bequest is only legislated for adults, analogous to divorce, manumission, gift, and loan.
The analogy (qiyas) with adult-only transactions is central: the minor lacks full legal capacity for such disposals.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i objection
The apparent benefit is not real because the minor's wealth is given away without need; true reward is attained when one sacrifices despite need. Also, the minor does not have full ownership rights over such transactions.
The exception of loan (qard) is mentioned: the judge can contract loan on behalf of the minor due to security against loss.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Hanafi: minor's bequest always void. Shafi'i: valid if for a clear benefit. This difference affects estate planning and guardianship.
The tarjih in this text favors the Hanafi position based on the strength of the analogy and the principle of afdal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَا إِذَا أَوْصَى الصَّبِيُّ بِشَيْءٍ مِنْ أَعْمَالِ الْبِرِّ بَطَلَتْ وَصِيَّتُهُ عِنْدَنَا خِلَافًا لِلشَّافِعِي وَإِنْ كَانَ فِيْهِ نَفْعٌ ظَاهِرٌ لِأَنَّ الْإِرْثَ شُرِعَ نَفْعًا لِلْمُوْرِثِ أَلَا تَرَىٰ أَنَّهُ شُرِعَ فِي حَقِّ الصَّبِيِّ وَفِي الْانْتِقَالِ عَنْهُ إِلَى الْإِيْصَاءِ تَرْكَ الْأَفْضَلِ لَا مَحَالَةَ إِلَّا أَنَّهُ شُرِعَ فِي حَقِّ الْبَالِغِ كَمَا شُرِعَ لَهُ الطَّلَاقُ وَالْعَتَاقُ وَالْهِبَةُ وَالْقَرْضُ وَلَمْ يُشْرَعْ ذَلِكَ فِي حَقِّ الصَّبِيِّ وَلَمْ يَمْلِكْ ذَلِكَ عَلَيْهِ غَيْرُهُ مَا خَلَا الْقَرْضَ فَإِنَّهُ يَمْلِكُهُ الْقَاضِي لِوُقُوعِ الْأَمْنِ عَنِ التَّلَفِ بِوِلَايَةِ الْقَضَاءِ
And as for when the minor bequeaths something from acts of piety, his bequest is void according to us, contrary to al-Shafi'i, even if there is an apparent benefit in it, because inheritance was legislated as a benefit for the testator. Do you not see that it was legislated for the minor, and in transferring from it to bequest there is necessarily leaving the superior. However, bequest is legislated for the adult just as divorce, manumission, gift, and loan are legislated for him, and those are not legislated for the minor, nor does anyone else own that on his behalf except the loan, for the judge owns it on his behalf due to security from loss by virtue of the authority of the judiciary.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on a minor's bequest (wasiyya) for pious acts (e.g., donating to a school) and contrasts it with the Shafi'i position. According to Hanafis, such a bequest is null (batil) even though outwardly it appears beneficial (thawab in the Hereafter). The proof: inheritance (irth) was legislated for the benefit of the testator (muwarith)—it provides reward through maintaining family ties. Transferring from inheritance to bequest means leaving what is superior (afdal) for what is inferior. Bequest is only legislated for adults, just as divorce, manumission, gift, and loan are adult prerogatives. The minor does not own the right to these transactions, and no one else can exercise them on his behalf except the loan, which the judge can issue because his authority provides security against loss. The Shafi'i objection (apparent benefit) is refuted by showing that the minor's wealth is given away without substitute, and the promised reward is not equivalent because the minor no longer needs the wealth after death.

Key Terms
وصية (wasiyya): A bequest or will made by a person regarding disposition of property after death.
إرث (irth): Inheritance, the transfer of property to heirs after death.
أفضل (afdal): The superior option; here, inheritance is considered afdal over bequest for the minor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do Hanafis rule a minor's bequest for a pious act void, despite its apparent benefit?
A) The minor cannot own property
B) Inheritance is already a greater benefit, and bequest is only for adults
C) The minor will not receive the reward
D) The guardian's permission is not given

Key Takeaway
A minor's bequest for pious acts is void in the Hanafi school because inheritance is the superior benefit, and bequest is an adult-only transaction.

Apostasy of a Minor: Binding Rulings and No Forgiveness
Stating Hanafi position on apostasy's consequences and the impossibility of forgiveness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that a ruling can be binding due to the validity of the act (sihha) rather than the actor's intention, and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa/Muhammad from Abu Yusuf in the case of a minor's apostasy.

Core Idea
The legal validity of an act itself, not the actor's intention, can trigger binding rulings; apostasy is effective even from a minor.

This lesson examines a key usul al-fiqh principle: how a ruling attaches to an act based on the act's inherent validity (sihha). The text from Al-Husami discusses apostasy by a minor: despite the minor's lack of full legal responsibility, the worldly and afterlife consequences of apostasy bind him. This is because the rulings follow from the act's validity, not from the intention behind it. We will unpack this principle, explore the intra-Hanafi difference with Abu Yusuf, and see how it applies in other areas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Legal Status
Focus on the act of apostasy (ridda) committed by a minor. Note that the text treats the act as legally valid (sahih) regardless of the minor's intention.
This is the starting point: the act is valid in itself.
2
Recognise the Basis of the Ruling
The text states 'فَإِنَّمَا يَلْزَمُهُ حُكْمًا لِصِحَتِهِ لَا قَصْدًا إِلَيْهِ' – the ruling binds as a consequence of the act's validity, not because of the agent's intention. Extract the usul principle: al-ahkam al-muta'alliqah bi al-fi'l al-sahih tathbut bi al-sihha la bi al-qasd.
This is the core usul principle.
3
Apply the Principle to the Case of a Minor
Apply the principle: since the minor's apostasy is valid, the full worldly rulings (e.g., execution, loss of property) and afterlife punishment are binding. Forgiveness (afw) is not possible because the ruling is not contingent on intention.
Contrast: if the ruling depended on intention, it might be waived.
4
Map the Intra-Madhhab Khilaf
Identify the difference: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the two) hold the binding view; Abu Yusuf requires intention for the worldly rulings to apply. Understand the thamarah al-khilaf – practical consequence in execution and inheritance.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.
5
Derive the Usul Maxim
From this case, formulate the general usul maxim: 'Rulings that are consequences of an act's validity (sihha) attach even when the agent lacks full intent.' Apply this maxim to other cases like the minor's conversion, contracts, or torts.
This maxim is an example of talil – reasoning by the effective cause (sihha).

وَأَمَّا الرِّدَةُ فَلَا تَحْتَمِلُ الْعَفْوَ فِي أَحْكَامِ الْآخِرَةِ وَمَا يَلْزَمُهُ مِنْ أَحْكَامِ الدُّنْيَا عِنْدَهُمَا خِلَافًا لِأَبِي يُوسُفَ فَإِنَّمَا يَلْزَمُهُ حُكْمًا لِصِحَتِهِ لَا قَصْدًا إِلَيْهِ فَلَمْ يَصِحَّ الْعَفْوُ عَنْ مِثْلِهِ كَمَا إِذَا ثَبَتَ تَبَعًا لِأَبَوَيْهِ
And as for apostasy, it does not admit forgiveness in the rulings of the Hereafter, and what binds him from the rulings of the world according to them (the two: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) contrary to Abu Yusuf, for it binds him as a ruling for its validity, not by intending it. So forgiveness of the like of it is not valid, just as when it is established by following one's parents.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage discusses apostasy (ridda) by a minor. The Hanafi position (held by Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani) is that apostasy does not admit of forgiveness (afw) in the matters of the Hereafter—meaning the punishment in the afterlife remains. As for the worldly rulings (e.g., execution, loss of property rights), they are binding on the apostate not because he intentionally committed apostasy with full legal capacity, but as a legal consequence (hukman) of the validity of the act itself. Therefore, forgiveness (e.g., by someone forgiving the apostasy or waiving punishment) is not valid. This is analogous to when a minor apostatizes by following his parents—the rulings still apply. Abu Yusuf differed, holding that the worldly rulings are not binding if the apostasy was not intentional. The key principle is that the minor's apostasy is effective in law despite his lack of full intention.

Key Terms
ردة (ridda): Apostasy from Islam; legally, it is the act of a Muslim renouncing the religion.
عفو (afw): Forgiveness or pardon; here, it means waiving the legal consequences of apostasy.
حكماً لصحته (hukman li sihhatihi): As a legal ruling arising from the validity of the act, not from the agent's intention.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position of the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), why are the worldly rulings of apostasy binding on a minor even without intention?
A) Because the minor is legally responsible (mukallaf)
B) Because the rulings follow from the validity of the act, not from intention
C) Because the guardian permitted it
D) Because the minor intended it

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul principle: an act's legal validity (sihha) alone can be the basis for attaching rulings, bypassing the need for intention in certain cases like a minor's apostasy.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
8 min
Child's Legal Capacity: Harmful Acts and the Exception of Apostasy
Explaining child's inability for harmful acts and rulings on will and apostasy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that a discerning child does not own transactions involving harm (darar) and state the exception for apostasy (riddah) with evidence from Quran and usul.

Core Idea
A child's will is void; his apostasy is valid — harm blocks capacity, but kufr overrides.

This passage from Sharh al-Husami deepens the usul principle that a child (sabi) cannot own acts where harm (darar) is possible. The author applies this to a child's will (wasiyyah) — void in Hanafi — and then addresses the seeming contradiction of apostasy (riddah), which is effective despite involving harm. We explore the Hanafi reasoning, the Shafi'i objection, and the Quranic proof that necessitates the exception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Principle
State the core Hanafi rule: a discerning child does not own any transaction that involves potential harm (darar). The child may become capable only with guardian permission.
This is the basis for all following applications.
2
Apply to Wasiyyah (Will)
Explain why a child's will is void: the child loses wealth without compensation (harm), and the reward is not considered because at death wealth is not needed. Even if reward existed, inheritance has greater benefit.
Contrast with the Shafi'i view: they see reward as pure benefit, so the will is valid.
3
Analyze the Hanafi Response to Shafi'i
Understand the two-part rebuttal: (a) the so-called benefit is illusory because the child does not sacrifice need; (b) inheritance is superior, so abandoning it is harmful.
The hadith about leaving heirs wealthy supports the preference for inheritance.
4
Examine the Exception: Riddah
Note that apostasy is effective in both worldly and otherworldly rulings despite harm. The reason is the Quranic text (4:48) that shirk cannot be forgiven. The worldly consequences (bainunah, disinheritance) are automatic, not imposed intentionally.
This distinguishes riddah from other harmful acts — the dalil overrides the general rule.
5
Derive the Usul Lesson
General rules (child's incapacity for harm) can have exceptions when a stronger evidence (qat'i text) demands it. This illustrates the Hanafi method of balancing principles.
The principle 'al-amm yufidu al-hukm' applies to the Quranic verse, creating an exception.

کے لوازم میں سے جو احکام ہیں وہ خود بخو دلازم ہو جا ئینگے جیسے بچہ کے ماں باپ مرتد ہو کر دار الحرب میں چلے جائیں تو ماں باپ کے تابع ہو کر ضمناً بچہ پر ارتداد کے احکام جاری ہوں گے ایسے ہی یہاں بھی احکام دنیا لازم ہوں گے۔
He (the child) will not be the owner of it except for giving a loan, for the judge becomes the owner of giving a loan due to safety from destruction because of the judge's authority. And in any case, apostasy does not admit of forgiveness in matters of the Hereafter, and those worldly rulings that are necessitated by it are agreed upon by the two parties, contrary to Imam Abu Yusuf, for he holds that they are necessary by the ruling of valid apostasy, not by intentional imposition. So forgiveness is not correct for a similar apostasy, just as apostasy is proven in following his parents. Previously it was stated that in transactions where there is possibility of benefit and harm, a child himself does not own them, but by the permission of the guardian the child becomes the owner. Hence, if a discerning child accepted agency (wakalah), the responsibility does not become necessary upon him, i.e., delivering the sold item or delivering the price, or litigating about a defect, because making these things obligatory upon the child involves harm. Therefore these things will be obligatory upon the principal (muwakkil) instead of the agent child. However, if he is doing it with the guardian's permission, then responsibility becomes necessary upon the child because the deficiency of the child's opinion is compensated by the guardian's permission, and the child becomes capable of this responsibility. And due to this possibility of harm, if a child makes a will (wasiyyah) from his wealth for a good deed, e.g., says: give my wealth to such-and-such madrasah, then according to us this will is void. Imam Shafi'i says that the will is valid because through the will for a good deed the child will obtain reward of the Hereafter, which is pure benefit for him, and it has been stated earlier that a child owns every transaction involving benefit, so he will also own the will. The author (al-Musannif al-Ali) is giving a response to Imam Shafi'i's proof: In the child's will, although apparently there is otherworldly benefit, but in reality there is no benefit because his wealth is being eliminated as a donation without compensation. As for the otherworldly benefit and reward, it is not considered because due to death the child becomes independent of wealth; at such a time spending his wealth in a good deed will not be a cause of reward. Complete reward in the Hereafter is obtained when this charity occurs at a time when he had complete need for wealth. Hence this is like a dead goat in the name of Allah, so this benefit will not be considered. "Because inheritance is legislated for benefit": And even if we concede the otherworldly benefit, still the will is void because inheritance is legislated for the benefit of the testator, since his heirs benefit from his wealth, which involves benefiting relatives and maintaining kinship ties, for which the testator has great reward. The hadith points to this: "Indeed it is better for you to leave your heirs wealthy than to leave them poor, begging from people" – the Prophet (صلى الله عليه وسلم) said to Sayyidina Sa'd (رضي الله عنه): "Your leaving your heirs wealthy is better than leaving them needy and stretching out their hands to people." In short, in inheritance there is benefit for the testator; therefore inheritance is legislated for a child, meaning that upon the child's death his heirs will inherit from him. If there were no benefit, inheritance would not be legislated for the child. It becomes clear that inheritance has more reward than a will because it involves both charity and maintaining kinship ties. Therefore, moving from inheritance to a will, i.e., depriving heirs and bequeathing to strangers, is abandoning the superior, and abandoning the superior involves harm, so the child's will is void. "But it is legislated for an adult": This is a response to a question: if a will is not permissible for a child due to harm, then it should also not be valid for an adult. The answer is that for an adult, divorce, manumission, gift-giving, and loan-giving are valid; similarly, an adult's will is valid. These things are not legislated for a child, meaning the child does not own them, neither by himself nor with guardian's permission, and neither guardian nor judge has authority over these matters for the child – i.e., if the guardian or judge divorces on behalf of the child, or frees a slave, or gives a gift, it will not be valid. However, if the judge loans someone the child's wealth, it is permissible because there is benefit for the child in that his wealth will be protected from loss; the judge, due to his authority, has the power to collect the loan; otherwise, if this wealth were an amanah (trust) with someone, it would not be guaranteed against destruction, which would harm the child. "As for apostasy, it does not admit of forgiveness": This is a response to a question: when all acts that involve harm to the child are not considered, then why is his apostasy considered in worldly and otherworldly rulings – e.g., his wife becomes bainunah (irrevocably divorced) from him, his Muslim relatives do not inherit from him – all these are harm upon the child, as the two parties (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) hold. Rather, apostasy should not be considered in worldly rulings, as Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Shafi'i hold; however, otherworldly rulings are effective according to all. The answer is that the effectiveness of apostasy in otherworldly rulings is because entering Paradise with disbelief and polytheism, and forgiveness of apostasy without repentance, is contrary to reason and contrary to the text: "Indeed Allah does not forgive that partners be associated with Him, but He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills" (Quran 4:48). As for the establishment of worldly rulings due to apostasy – like the wife becoming bainunah, deprivation of inheritance, etc. – although they involve harm for the child, these rulings are not intentionally imposed upon him; rather, they follow the validity of apostasy and are necessitated implicitly. That is, when the child's apostasy is valid and cannot be forgiven without repentance, then the rulings that are necessary consequences of apostasy occur automatically, just as if a child's parents apostatize and go to dar al-harb, the rulings of apostasy apply to the child implicitly by following his parents; similarly here worldly rulings become necessary.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Husami discusses the legal capacity of a child (sabi) regarding acts that involve potential harm (darar). The author establishes a key principle: a child is not the owner of any transaction that involves possibility of harm, unless permitted by the guardian. This principle is then applied to several cases: agency (wakalah), will (wasiyyah), and apostasy (riddah). 

First, regarding agency: if a discerning child accepts agency, the responsibilities (delivery, litigation) do not fall on him because they would cause harm; they fall on the principal. If the guardian permits, then the child becomes capable because the guardian compensates for the child's deficient judgment.

Second, regarding a child's will: the Hanafi position is that it is void. Imam Shafi'i argues it is valid because the child seeks reward, which is pure benefit. The commentary refutes this by saying: (a) the child loses wealth without compensation, which is harm; the reward is not considered because at the time of death the child no longer needs wealth, so spending it does not count as a sacrifice; (b) even if reward were considered, inheritance takes precedence because it benefits both heirs and testator, and abandoning the superior (inheritance) for the inferior (will) is harmful. The hadith about leaving heirs wealthy supports this.

Third, a distinction between child and adult: an adult can make a will because he also can divorce, manumit, etc., which are not allowed for a child. The child lacks ownership over these harmful acts entirely, even by guardian. An exception is when a judge loans the child's money to protect it from loss.

Fourth, regarding apostasy: a child's apostasy is effective in both worldly and otherworldly rulings, despite the harm. The reason is that apostasy is an act of kufr that cannot be forgiven without repentance. The worldly consequences (divorce, disinheritance) are not imposed as punishments but are necessary accompaniments of valid apostasy. This is analogous to a child automatically following his apostate parents. The opposing view of Abu Yusuf and Shafi'i (that worldly effects do not apply) is rejected because the text and reason demand that apostasy has consequences.

The commentary thus reinforces the Hanafi principle that a child's acts involving harm are invalid, but apostasy is an exception because its nature as kufr overrides the general rule. This shows the nuanced approach of Hanafi usul: general rules may have exceptions when a stronger dalil (Quranic text on shirk) or necessity dictates.

Key Terms
صبی (Sabi): A minor child who is discerning (عاقل) but not yet adult; lacks full legal capacity.
وصیت (Wasiyyah): A will bequeathing property after death; for a child, it is invalid in Hanafi.
ردت (Riddah): Apostasy from Islam; for a child, it is effective in all rulings according to Hanafi (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad).
ضرر (Darar): Harm or loss; child cannot perform acts where darar is possible.
ولایت (Wilayah): Guardianship; gives the guardian authority to permit child's acts or, for a judge, to give loans.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the reason a child's will (wasiyyah) is void?
A) The child does not own any property at all.
B) The child loses wealth without compensation (harm) and inheritance is more beneficial.
C) A child cannot make contracts without guardian permission.
D) The child is too young to understand the meaning of a will.

Key Takeaway
A child does not own harmful acts, but apostasy is an exception because its gravity (shirk) overrides the general rule, as proven by the Quranic text.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
12 min
Harm and Capacity: Child’s Bequest and Apostasy
Discussion on validity of child's wasiyyah and riddah based on harm principle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule distinguishing primary and incidental harm to determine the validity of a child’s legal acts, such as bequest and apostasy.

Core Idea
Incidental harm does not invalidate a valid act; primary harm does.

This passage explores a key usul al-fiqh principle: the role of harm (ḍarar) in evaluating the validity of a child's actions. We examine two contrasting cases: a child's bequest (waṣiyyah), which is invalid because it inflicts primary harm on the heirs, and a child's apostasy (riddah), which, despite causing harm, is considered valid in worldly rulings because the harm is incidental to the act itself. The discussion clarifies how legal capacity (ahliyyah) and the nature of harm interact in Hanafi jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and their harm assessment
In the passage, two acts by a child are discussed: bequest (waṣiyyah) and apostasy (riddah). Determine whether each act is deemed harmful and how the harm is classified (primary or incidental).
Bequest is considered harmful because it deprives heirs of their rights. Apostasy leads to worldly harms like marriage dissolution and loss of inheritance.
2
Distinguish primary vs incidental harm
The passage argues that bequest involves primary harm (ḍarar maqṣūd), so it is invalid from a child. Apostasy's harm is incidental (tabʿī) – it follows from the act's validity, not intended as the purpose. Note the difference in reasoning.
Primary harm invalidates; incidental harm does not if the act itself is valid.
3
Apply the capacity principle
An adult has full legal capacity (ahliyyat al-adāʾ) and can perform harmful acts. A child lacks this capacity for acts where harm is primary. Check how the passage contrasts adult and child capacity for bequest, divorce, manumission, etc.
Adults are allowed harmful acts because they own the right to dispose. Children do not.
4
Trace the incidental harm argument for apostasy
The passage answers an objection: if harmful acts are invalid, why is apostasy valid? The answer: the worldly rulings (separation of wife, inheritance changes) are not intended primary obligations but incidental consequences (luwāzim) that follow automatically from the validity of apostasy itself. Thus, the harm is secondary.
This is supported by the Quranic verse (4:48) that apostasy incurs punishment in the afterlife, underscoring its validity.

الاانه شرع فى حق البالغ : - ایک سوال کا جواب ہے جس کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ وصیت کرنا ضر ر کی وجہ سے بچہ کے لئے جائز نہیں ہے، تو پھر بالغ کے لئے بھی وصیت درست نہ ہونی چاہیے۔
اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ بالغ کی طلاق ، عتاق اور ہبہ دینا اور قرض دینا درست ہے، اسی طرح بالغ کی وصیت بھی درست ہے، اور یہ چیزیں بچہ کے لئے مشروع نہیں ہیں، یعنی بچہ ان کا مالک نہیں ہے، نہ خود نہ ولی کی اجازت سے، اور نہ ولی یا قاضی بچہ پر ان امور کا مالک ہوگا، یعنی ولی یا قاضی بچہ کی طرف سے طلاق دے، یا غلام آزاد کرے یا مال ہبہ کرے تو جائز نہ ہوگا۔ البتہ قاضی اگر بچہ کا مال کسی کو قرض دے تو جائز ہے، کیونکہ اس میں بچہ کا فائدہ ہے کہ اس کا مال ضائع ہونے سے محفوظ رہے گا ، اور قاضی ولایت کی وجہ سے قرض کو وصول کرنے کی طاقت رکھتا ہے ورنہ اگر یہ مال کسی کے پاس امانت ہو تو ہلاکت سے ضمان نہیں آئے گا جس میں بچہ کا ضرر ہے۔
واما الردة فلا تحتمل :- ایک سوال کا جواب ہے، جس کی تقریر یہ ہے کہ جب بچہ سے وہ تمام افعال غیر معتبر ہیں جن میں اس کا ضر ر ہے تو پھر اس کی ردت دنیوی اور اخروی احکام میں کیوں معتبر ہے، جیسے اس کی بیوی اس سے بائنہ ہو جاتی ہے، اس کے مسلمان رشتہ دار اس کے وارث نہیں ہوتے ، یہ بچہ کے لئے ضرر ہی ضرر ہیں ، جیسے کہ اس کے قائل ہیں طرفین ، بلکہ ردت کا دینوی احکام میں اعتبار نہ ہونا چاہیے جیسے کہ امام ابو یوسف اور امام شافعی اس کے قائل ہیں ، البتہ احکام آخرت تو سب کے نزدیک نافذ ہوں گے، اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ احکام آخرت میں ردت کا نافذ ہونا اس لئے ہے کہ کفر و شرک کے ساتھ جنت کا داخلہ اور بغیر تو بہ کے ارتداد کی معافی خلاف عقل بھی ہے اور خلاف نص بھی ہے، ان الله لا یغفران شرك به ویغفر ما دون ذلك لمن يشاء
اور ردت سے احکام دنیا کا ثبوت جیسے بیوی کا بائنہ ہونا، حرمان میراث وغیرہ تو ان میں اگر چہ بچہ کا ضرر ہے لیکن یہ احکام اس پر قصد الازم نہیں کئے گئے بلکہ صحت ارتداد کے تابع ہو کر ضمنالازم ہوئے ہیں، یعنی جب بچہ کا ارتداد صحیح ہے اور بغیر تو بہ کے اس کی معافی نہیں ہے تو ارتداد کے لوازم میں سے جو احکام ہیں وہ خود بخو دلازم ہو جا ئینگے جیسے بچہ کے ماں باپ مرتد ہو کر دار الحرب میں چلے جائیں تو ماں باپ کے تابع ہو کر ضمناً بچہ پر ارتداد کے احکام جاری ہوں گے ایسے ہی یہاں بھی احکام دنیا لازم ہوں گے۔
(Continued from previous) ...the heirs will be his heirs. If there were no benefit, inheritance would not be legislated for the child. It became known that inheritance has more reward than bequest because it includes charity and maintaining family ties. Therefore, shifting from inheritance to bequest, i.e., depriving the heirs and bequeathing to strangers, is abandoning what is better. Abandoning what is better involves harm, so the child's bequest is invalid.
"Except that it is legislated for the adult" — This is an answer to a question. The gist is: If bequest is not permissible for a child due to harm, then it should also not be valid for an adult. The answer is that for an adult, divorce, manumission, gift, and loan are valid. Similarly, his bequest is valid. These things are not legislated for a child; the child does not own them — neither himself nor by guardian's permission. Neither a guardian nor a judge has the right to do these things on behalf of the child: e.g., if a guardian or judge divorces on behalf of the child, frees a slave, or gives a gift, it is not valid. However, if a judge lends the child's property to someone, it is permissible because the child benefits by preserving his wealth from loss, and the judge, due to his authority, has the power to recover the loan. Otherwise, if this wealth were an amānat (trust) with someone, it would be lost without guarantee, which would harm the child.
"As for apostasy, it is not subject [to this rule]" — This is an answer to another question. The elaboration: Since all actions that involve harm are invalid for the child, why is his apostasy considered in worldly and otherworldly rulings? For example, his wife becomes irrevocably separated, his Muslim relatives do not inherit from him — these are certainly harmful. So apostasy should not be considered in worldly rulings, as Imām Abū Yūsuf and Imām al-Shāfiʿī hold. But otherworldly rulings are effective according to all. The answer is that apostasy's effectiveness in the afterlife is because entering Paradise with disbelief and polytheism, and forgiving apostasy without repentance, is contrary to reason and text: "Indeed, Allah does not forgive association with Him, but He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills" (Quran 4:48). As for the establishment of worldly rulings due to apostasy — like separation of wife, deprivation of inheritance etc. — although these involve harm, these rulings are not intended as primary obligations for the child. Rather, they are incidental consequences following the soundness of apostasy. That is, when a child's apostasy is valid and without repentance it is not forgiven, then the rulings that are necessary consequences of apostasy automatically apply. For example, if a child's parents apostatize and go to dār al-ḥarb, then following the parents, the rulings of apostasy apply to the child incidentally. Similarly here, worldly rulings become necessary.
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage is a continued discussion on the principle of harm (ḍarar) and legal capacity. The author addresses two objections: (1) Why is a child's bequest (waṣiyyah) invalid while an adult's is valid? (2) Why is a child's apostasy (riddah) considered in worldly rulings despite harm? The first is answered by distinguishing the adult's full legal capacity (ahliyyah adāʾ) which permits harmful acts, whereas the child lacks such capacity. The second is answered by stating that apostasy's worldly effects are not primary obligations but incidental consequences (luwāzim) that follow from the validity of apostasy itself. The author cites the Quranic verse on shirk and mentions the opinion of Imām Abū Yūsuf and Imām al-Shāfiʿī that apostasy should not have worldly effects for a child, but the majority (Hanafis) hold it valid due to the principle of subsidiary rulings (tabʿiyyah).

Key Terms
ضرر (ḍarar): Harm that invalidates an action if the action is primarily intended; if harm is incidental, the action may still be valid.
ردۃ (riddah): Apostasy from Islam; considered valid from a minor as a subsidiary ruling following the validity of the act itself.
وصیت (waṣiyyah): Bequest; for a child, it is invalid due to harm because it deprives heirs of their right better than charity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is a child's apostasy considered valid in worldly rulings despite causing harm?
A) Because harm is not considered for children.
B) Because the worldly rulings are incidental consequences of the valid apostasy, not primary obligations.
C) Because Imām Abū Yūsuf and Imām al-Shāfiʿī agree it is valid.
D) Because the child can repent later.

Key Takeaway
A child's apostasy triggers worldly rulings because its harm is incidental, not primary, and thus does not invalidate the act.

Classification of Impediments to Legal Capacity
Lists and defines types of impediments (awāriḍ) affecting legal capacity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify impediments (awāriḍ) into samāwī and muktasab, and further subdivide muktasab into those from oneself and from others.

Core Idea
Impediments to capacity are either heavenly (samāwī) or acquired (muktasab); acquired come from oneself or external coercion.

This lesson introduces the first part of the chapter where the author categorizes all impediments that can affect a person's legal capacity. Understanding this classification is essential for later detailed rulings on each impediment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main categories
Read the first sentence: 'al-ʿawāriḍu nawʿān: samāwī wa muktasab'. Recognize that the entire classification is based on the origin of the impediment.
Samāwī = heavenly, beyond human control; muktasab = acquired, involves human action.
2
List the samāwī impediments
The text enumerates ten: ṣughr (minority), junūn (insanity), ʿatah (mental weakness), nisyān (forgetfulness), nawm (sleep), ighmā (unconsciousness), riqq (slavery), marad (illness), ḥayḍ (menstruation), nifās (postpartum bleeding), and mawt (death). Commit them to memory.
Slavery is considered heavenly because it is not chosen by the individual in classical fiqh.
3
Subdivide muktasab into nafsī and ghayrī
Muktasab has two sources: from oneself (min nafsihi) and from another (min ghayrihi). The text lists: from oneself: jahl (ignorance), safah (foolishness), sukr (intoxication), hazl (jest), khaṭa (error), safar (travel). From another: ikrāh (coercion), with or without compulsion.
Coercion (ikrāh) is the only example of muktasab min ghayrihi given here.
4
Note the purpose of classification
The classification sets the stage for detailed rules: each impediment has specific effects on capacity and obligations. For example, samāwī impediments may drop obligations entirely if prolonged, while muktasab may only suspend or modify them.

Source Text

شرح اردو المنتخب الحسامي
اَلْعَوَارِضُ نَوْعَانِ سَمَاوِيٌّ وَمُكْتَسَبْ اَمَّا السَّمَاوِيُّ فَهُوَ الصَّغْرُ وَالْجُنُونُ وَالعَتَهُ وَالنِّسْيانُ وَالنَّوْمُ وَالْإغْمَاء وَالرّقُ وَالْمَرَضُ وَالْحَيْضُ وَالنَّفَاسُ وَالْمَوْتُ وَاَمَّا الْمَكْتَسَبْ فَنَوْعَانِ مِنْهُ وَمِنْ غَيْرِهِ لَمَّا الَّذِي مِنْهُ فَالْجَهْلُ وَالسَّفَهُ وَالسُّكْرَ وَالْهَزْلُ وَالْخَطَأُ وَ السَّفَرُ وَاَمَّا الَّذِي مِنْ غَيْرِهِ فَالْإِكْرَاهُ بِمَا فِيْهِ الْجَاء وَبِمَا لَيْسَ فِيهِ الْجَاء وَاَمَّا الْجُنُونُ فَإِنَّهُ يُوجِبُ الْحَجْرَ عَنِ الْأَقْوَال وَيَسْقُطُ بِهِ مَا كَانَ ضَرَرًا يَحْتَمِلُ السُّقُوطَ وَإِذَا امْتَدَ فَصَارَ لُزُوْمُ الْأَدَاءِ يُؤَدِئُ إلَى الْحَرَج فَيَبْطُلُ الْقَوْلُ بِالْآدَاءِ وَيَنْعَدِمُ الْوُجُوْب أَيْضًا لِإِنْعِدَامِهِ وَحَدُ الْإِمْتِدَادِ فِي الصَّوْمِ أَن يَسْتَوْعِبَ الشَّهْرَ وَفي الصَّلَواتِ أَن يَزِيدَ عَلَى يَوْمٍ وَلَيْلَةٍ وفِي الزَّكَوةِ أَنْ يَسْتَوْعِبَ الْحَوْلَ عِنْدَ مُحَمَّدِ اللهِ وَأَقَامَ أَبُويُوسُفَ الالي اَكْثَرَ الْحَوْل مَقَام كُلِهِ تَيْسِيرًا وَمَا كَانَ حَسَنًا لَا يَحْتَمِلُ الْغَيْرَ أَوْ قَبِيْحًا لَا يَحْتَمِلُ الْعَفْوَ فَثَابِتٌ فِى حَقَّهِ حَتَّى يَثْبِتَ أَيْمَانُهُ وَرِدَّتُهُ تَبْعَا لَا بَوَيْهِ - ترجمہ : یہ فصل ان امور کے بیان میں ہے جو اہلیت پر عارض ہوتے ہیں ،عوارض دو قسم کے ہیں ،سماوی اور کیسی ، بہر حال سماوی پس وہ صغر، جنون ، عته ، ( فتور عقل ) نسیان ، نیند ، ہوشی، غلامی ، بیماری حیض ونفاس ، اور موت ہے، اور بہر حال کسی اس کی دوقسمیں ہیں، ایک بندہ کی طرف سے اور دوسرے اس کے غیر کی طرف سے، بہر حال وہ جو بندے کی طرف سے ہیں پس وہ جہل ہے اور سفہ ( خفت عقل ) ہے، اور سکر، ہنرل ، خطاء اور سفر ہے، اور بہر حال وہ جو اس کے غیر کی طرف سے ہیں تو وہ اکراہ ہے ایسی چیز کے ساتھ جس میں الجاء ہو، اور ایسی چیز کے ساتھ جس میں الجاء نہ ہو، اور بہر حال جنون پس وہ اقوال سے حجر کو واجب کرتا ہے، اور جنون کی وجہ سے وہ ضر ر ساقط ہو جاتا ہے جو سقوط کا احتمال رکھتا ہے، اور جب جنون ممتد ہو جائے تو اداء کا لزوم حرج تک پہنچا دے گا ، تو لزوم اداء کا قول باطل ہو جائے گا ، اور اداء کے معدوم ہونے سے نفسِ وجوب بھی معدوم ہو جائے گا، اور روزہ میں امتداد کی حد یہ ہے کہ جنون مہینہ کو گھیر لے اور نمازوں میں یہ ہے کہ جنون ایک دن رات سے بڑھ جائے ، اور زکوۃ میں یہ ہے کہ جنون سال کو گھیر لے امام محمد عالی کے نزدیک ، اور امام ابو یوسف ہے نے آسانی کے لئے اکثر حول کو کل کے قائم مقام کیا ہے، اور جو فعل ایسا نے حسن ہو جو غیر کا احتمال نہ رکھے یا ایسا قبیح ہو جو عفو کا احتمال نہ رکھے تو وہ مجنون کے حق میں ثابت ہوگا ، یہاں تک کہ اس کا
Chapter on the matters that impede legal capacity.
Urdu commentary of Al-Muntakhab Al-Husami.
The impediments are of two types: celestial and acquired. As for the celestial, they are: minority, insanity, feeble-mindedness, forgetfulness, sleep, unconsciousness, slavery, illness, menstruation, postpartum bleeding, and death. As for the acquired, they are of two types: from oneself and from another. As for that which is from oneself: ignorance, foolishness, intoxication, jest, error, and travel. As for that which is from another: coercion, whether involving compulsion or not.
As for insanity: it necessitates interdiction from speech acts, and by it drops what is harmful and can possibly drop. When it becomes prolonged, so that the necessity of performance leads to hardship, then the claim of performance becomes invalid, and obligation itself ceases due to its non-existence. The limit of prolongation for fasting is that it encompasses the month; for prayers, that it exceeds a day and a night; for zakah, that it encompasses the year according to Imam Muhammad. Abu Yusuf established the majority of the year in place of the whole for convenience. Whatever is good that cannot be other than good, or evil that cannot be forgiven, is established in his right, so that his oaths and his apostasy are established as subsidiary, not primarily.
Translation: This chapter is about the matters that affect capacity. Impediments are two types: celestial and acquired. As for celestial, they are: minority, insanity, feeble-mindedness (weakness of intellect), forgetfulness, sleep, unconsciousness, slavery, illness, menstruation, postpartum bleeding, and death. As for acquired, it has two types: from the person and from other than him. As for what is from the person: ignorance, foolishness (lightness of intellect), intoxication, jest, error, and travel. As for what is from other than him: coercion, with compulsion and without compulsion. As for insanity: it necessitates interdiction from speech acts, and by it drops that harm which can possibly drop. When insanity becomes prolonged, the necessity of performance leads to hardship, so the claim of performance becomes invalid, and with the non-existence of performance, the very obligation ceases. The limit of prolongation for fasting is that insanity covers the month; for prayers, that it exceeds a day and a night; for zakah, that it covers the year according to Imam Muhammad. Imam Abu Yusuf put the majority of the year in place of the whole for convenience. An action that is good and cannot be otherwise, or evil that cannot be forgiven, is established in his right, even to the extent that his oaths and apostasy are established subsidiary, not primarily.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage opens a new chapter on impediments (awāriḍ) that affect legal capacity (ahliyyah). The author first classifies impediments into two broad categories: celestial (samāwī) — those beyond human control — and acquired (muktasab) — those arising from human action. The celestial list includes childhood, insanity, mental weakness, forgetfulness, sleep, etc. The acquired list is further subdivided into those arising from oneself (like ignorance, foolishness, intoxication) and those from external sources (like coercion).

جنون (junūn): Insanity; causes interdiction from verbal acts and drops obligations when prolonged.
حجر (ḥajr): Interdiction; preventing a person from executing certain legal acts due to deficiency.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
8 min
Legal Effects of Insanity (Junūn) on Capacity
Details the legal effects of insanity (junūn) as an impediment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi rules on how prolonged insanity drops obligations, specify the duration thresholds for different acts, and identify the subsidiary validity of purely good or evil acts.

Core Idea
Prolonged insanity lifts obligations causing hardship; specific durations for fasting, prayer, and zakāt determine when this occurs.

This lesson focuses on the detailed discussion of insanity (junūn) as an impediment. The text explains that insanity leads to interdiction from verbal acts, can drop harmful obligations, and when prolonged, removes the obligation itself. It also defines the exact durations for different acts and notes that purely good or evil acts remain valid in the insane person's right.

Guided Path
1
Understand the primary effect of insanity
Insanity necessitates ḥajr (interdiction) from verbal acts (aqwāl) and drops obligations that involve harm and can be dropped. This means the insane person cannot perform valid spoken contracts or utterances, and certain duties fall away.
Not all obligations drop; only those entailing hardship and with potential for removal.
2
Identify the condition for complete removal of obligation
When insanity is prolonged (imtadda), the necessity of performance (luzūm al-adāʾ) leads to hardship (ḥaraj), so the obligation itself (wujūb) ceases. The text then defines the limits of prolongation for fasting, prayer, and zakāh.
For fasting: insanity covers the whole month; for prayer: exceeds a day and night; for zakāh: covers the whole year (Imam Muḥammad) or most of the year (Abū Yūsuf).
3
Distinguish between positions of Imām Muḥammad and Imām Abū Yūsuf
Imām Muḥammad requires the full year for zakāh obligation to drop; Imām Abū Yūsuf deems the majority (akthar al-ḥawl) sufficient for ease. Note the principle of taysīr (facilitation).
This intra-Hanafi ikhtilāf affects the fiqh ruling on zakāh for the insane.
4
Recognize the exception for purely good or evil acts
Acts that are purely good (i.e., cannot be other than good) or purely evil (cannot be forgiven) remain valid even for the insane. Thus, his oaths (aymān) and apostasy (riddah) are established as subsidiary effects (tabʿan), not primarily.
These acts do not depend on capacity; they are legally recognized regardless of sanity.

Source Text

شرح اردو المنتخب الحسامي

ترجمہ : یہ فصل ان امور کے بیان میں ہے جو اہلیت پر عارض ہوتے ہیں ،عوارض دو قسم کے ہیں ،سماوی اور کیسی ، بہر حال سماوی پس وہ صغر، جنون ، عته ، ( فتور عقل ) نسیان ، نیند ، ہوشی، غلامی ، بیماری حیض ونفاس ، اور موت ہے، اور بہر حال کسی اس کی دوقسمیں ہیں، ایک بندہ کی طرف سے اور دوسرے اس کے غیر کی طرف سے، بہر حال وہ جو بندے کی طرف سے ہیں پس وہ جہل ہے اور سفہ ( خفت عقل ) ہے، اور سکر، ہنرل ، خطاء اور سفر ہے، اور بہر حال وہ جو اس کے غیر کی طرف سے ہیں تو وہ اکراہ ہے ایسی چیز کے ساتھ جس میں الجاء ہو، اور ایسی چیز کے ساتھ جس میں الجاء نہ ہو، اور بہر حال جنون پس وہ اقوال سے حجر کو واجب کرتا ہے، اور جنون کی وجہ سے وہ ضر ر ساقط ہو جاتا ہے جو سقوط کا احتمال رکھتا ہے، اور جب جنون ممتد ہو جائے تو اداء کا لزوم حرج تک پہنچا دے گا ، تو لزوم اداء کا قول باطل ہو جائے گا ، اور اداء کے معدوم ہونے سے نفسِ وجوب بھی معدوم ہو جائے گا، اور روزہ میں امتداد کی حد یہ ہے کہ جنون مہینہ کو گھیر لے اور نمازوں میں یہ ہے کہ جنون ایک دن رات سے بڑھ جائے ، اور زکوۃ میں یہ ہے کہ جنون سال کو گھیر لے امام محمد عالی کے نزدیک ، اور امام ابو یوسف ہے نے آسانی کے لئے اکثر حول کو کل کے قائم مقام کیا ہے، اور جو فعل ایسا نے حسن ہو جو غیر کا احتمال نہ رکھے یا ایسا قبیح ہو جو عفو کا احتمال نہ رکھے تو وہ مجنون کے حق میں ثابت ہوگا ، یہاں تک کہ اس کا
Chapter on the matters that impede legal capacity.
Urdu commentary of Al-Muntakhab Al-Husami.
The impediments are of two types: celestial and acquired. As for the celestial, they are: minority, insanity, feeble-mindedness, forgetfulness, sleep, unconsciousness, slavery, illness, menstruation, postpartum bleeding, and death. As for the acquired, they are of two types: from oneself and from another. As for that which is from oneself: ignorance, foolishness, intoxication, jest, error, and travel. As for that which is from another: coercion, whether involving compulsion or not.
As for insanity: it necessitates interdiction from speech acts, and by it drops what is harmful and can possibly drop. When it becomes prolonged, so that the necessity of performance leads to hardship, then the claim of performance becomes invalid, and obligation itself ceases due to its non-existence. The limit of prolongation for fasting is that it encompasses the month; for prayers, that it exceeds a day and a night; for zakah, that it encompasses the year according to Imam Muhammad. Abu Yusuf established the majority of the year in place of the whole for convenience. Whatever is good that cannot be other than good, or evil that cannot be forgiven, is established in his right, so that his oaths and his apostasy are established as subsidiary, not primarily.
Translation: This chapter is about the matters that affect capacity. Impediments are two types: celestial and acquired. As for celestial, they are: minority, insanity, feeble-mindedness (weakness of intellect), forgetfulness, sleep, unconsciousness, slavery, illness, menstruation, postpartum bleeding, and death. As for acquired, it has two types: from the person and from other than him. As for what is from the person: ignorance, foolishness (lightness of intellect), intoxication, jest, error, and travel. As for what is from other than him: coercion, with compulsion and without compulsion. As for insanity: it necessitates interdiction from speech acts, and by it drops that harm which can possibly drop. When insanity becomes prolonged, the necessity of performance leads to hardship, so the claim of performance becomes invalid, and with the non-existence of performance, the very obligation ceases. The limit of prolongation for fasting is that insanity covers the month; for prayers, that it exceeds a day and a night; for zakah, that it covers the year according to Imam Muhammad. Imam Abu Yusuf put the majority of the year in place of the whole for convenience. An action that is good and cannot be otherwise, or evil that cannot be forgiven, is established in his right, even to the extent that his oaths and apostasy are established subsidiary, not primarily.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

Insanity and Legal Capacity: The Rule of Hajr and Imtidad
Rule on insanity's effect on capacity and worship obligations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on insanity's effect on verbal acts and worship obligations, and apply the condition of prolongation (imtidad) to fasting and prayer.

Core Idea
Prolonged insanity removes obligations that admit lapse; verbal acts are restricted; faith remains.

This passage from Al-Husami deals with the legal capacity (ahliyyah) of an insane person. Insanity (junūn) is a partial impediment: it restricts verbal acts (like divorce, gift) but only drops obligations that can lawfully lapse due to hardship, provided the insanity is prolonged. The text details the minimum durations for fasting, prayer, and zakat. Understanding these limits is crucial for determining whether missed acts must be made up later.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two effects of insanity
The text states two consequences: first, 'ḥajr ʿan al-aqwāl' – a legal restriction that invalidates any verbal disposition (e.g., sale, divorce, manumission) by the insane person. Second, suqūṭ (fall) of obligations that are 'harmful and admit fall' – i.e., acts of worship that can legally lapse due to valid excuses.
Worship obligations like prayer, fasting, zakat are considered 'harmful' in the sense of requiring effort; they can drop when genuine hardship exists.
2
Understand the condition of prolongation (imtidad)
The mere occurrence of insanity does not automatically drop obligations. It must be prolonged (imtadā). The reason: insisting on performance after recovery would constitute hardship (ḥaraj), and the Shariʿah removes hardship. The obligation drops only when prolongation would make the demand burdensome.
The principle is based on Qurʾānic rulings like 'lā yukallifu Llāhu nafsan illā wusʿahā'.
3
Apply the specific duration limits
The text sets clear thresholds: for fasting (ṣawm), insanity must encompass the entire month (i.e., the full lunar month). For prayers (ṣalawāt), it must exceed one full day and night (i.e., more than 24 hours). For zakat, there is a difference between Imām Muḥammad and Abū Yūsuf.
These limits are not arbitrary; they reflect the nature of each obligation – fasting is a continuous month, prayer is daily, zakat is annual.
4
Note the exception for pure good and pure evil
Acts that are purely good (like īmān, faith) or purely evil (like riddah, apostasy) are not affected by insanity. They are established in the person's right as a consequence of his original capacity, not independently. Thus, if an insane person previously had faith, he remains a believer even if he cannot manifest it.
This preserves the inviolability of one's religious status despite the loss of rational capacity.
5
Recognize the intra-Hanafi difference on zakat
While Imām Muḥammad insists on the entire year (al-ḥawl) being covered for zakat to drop, Abū Yūsuf accepts most of the year (akthar al-ḥawl) as sufficient, citing ease (taysīr). This is a practical concession and shows that even within the school, variations exist based on underlying principles.
No tarjīḥ is given here, but Abū Yūsuf's view is often relied upon in fatwā for convenience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَاَمَّا الْجُنُونُ فَإِنَّهُ يُوجِبُ الْحَجْرَ عَنِ الْأَقْوَال وَيَسْقُطُ بِهِ مَا كَانَ ضَرَرًا يَحْتَمِلُ السُّقُوطَ وَإِذَا امْتَدَ فَصَارَ لُزُوْمُ الْأَدَاءِ يُؤَدِئُ إلَى الْحَرَج فَيَبْطُلُ الْقَوْلُ بِالْآدَاءِ وَيَنْعَدِمُ الْوُجُوْب أَيْضًا لِإِنْعِدَامِهِ وَحَدُ الْإِمْتِدَادِ فِي الصَّوْمِ أَن يَسْتَوْعِبَ الشَّهْرَ وَفي الصَّلَواتِ أَن يَزِيدَ عَلَى يَوْمٍ وَلَيْلَةٍ وفِي الزَّكَوةِ أَنْ يَسْتَوْعِبَ الْحَوْلَ عِنْدَ مُحَمَّدِ اللهِ وَأَقَامَ أَبُويُوسُفَ الالي اَكْثَرَ الْحَوْل مَقَام كُلِهِ تَيْسِيرًا وَمَا كَانَ حَسَنًا لَا يَحْتَمِلُ الْغَيْرَ أَوْ قَبِيْحًا لَا يَحْتَمِلُ الْعَفْوَ فَثَابِتٌ فِى حَقَّهِ حَتَّى يَثْبِتَ أَيْمَانُهُ وَرِدَّتُهُ تَبْعَا لَا بَوَيْهِ
As for insanity, it necessitates restriction from verbal [acts] and by it falls whatever is a harm that admits fall. And when it becomes prolonged, the necessity of performance leads to hardship, so the statement of performance becomes void and the obligation also ceases because of its ceasing. The limit of prolongation in fasting is that it encompasses the month, in prayers that it exceeds a day and night, and in zakat that it encompasses the year according to Muhammad. Abu Yusuf made most of the year stand in place of the entire year for ease. And what is good that does not admit otherwise or evil that does not admit pardon is established in his right, so that his faith and apostasy are established as a consequence, not by itself.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling on insanity (junūn) as an impediment to legal capacity. Insanity causes ḥajr (restriction) concerning verbal acts (aqwāl), meaning the insane person's statements (like divorce, manumission, gift) are ineffective. However, harmful acts that admit fall—such as acts that can lapse due to valid excuses—are removed by insanity. When insanity is prolonged (imtidād), insisting on performance of obligations leads to hardship (ḥaraj), so the obligation of performance drops, and the underlying obligation itself ceases. The text specifies the extent of prolongation: for fasting, insanity must cover the entire month; for prayers, more than a day and night; for zakat, the entire year according to Imam Muhammad, while Abu Yusuf considers most of the year sufficient for ease. Acts that are purely good (like belief) or purely evil (like apostasy) remain established for the insane person, so his faith or apostasy (if he manifested it) is considered effective, but only as a consequence of his parents' status (if he was a child following them), not independently. This ruling balances the removal of hardship while preserving core religious status.

Key Terms
الْحَجْر (al-ḥajr): Legal restriction preventing the validity of verbal acts of an insane person.
امْتِدَاد (imtidād): Prolongation; the duration of insanity necessary to trigger the fall of obligations.
الْحَرَج (al-ḥaraj): Hardship; the principle that obligation is lifted when leading to undue difficulty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the limit of prolongation for insanity to remove the obligation of fasting?
A) It must exceed a day and night
B) It must encompass the entire month
C) It must encompass the entire year
D) Most of the month suffices

Key Takeaway
Prolonged insanity drops worship obligations that admit lapse, but only when the prolongation meets specific limits for each act; faith remains unaffected.

The Zakat Obligation During Prolonged Insanity: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on zakat duration for insanity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two Hanafi positions on the duration of insanity required to drop zakat, and apply the thamarah al-khilaf to a practical scenario.

Core Idea
Imam Muhammad requires full year of insanity to drop zakat; Abu Yusuf accepts most of the year for ease.

In the previous lesson, we studied the general rule of insanity's effect. Here we zoom into the specific case of zakat, where two senior Hanafi imams—Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf—differ on the required duration. This lesson unpacks their proofs and the practical difference, illustrating how the principle of taysir (ease) operates within the school.

Guided Path
1
State the two positions
Imam Muhammad: for zakat to be dropped, the insanity must encompass the entire zakaah year (al-ḥawl kullihi). Abu Yusuf: most of the year (akthar al-ḥawl) is sufficient, substituting for the entire year.
The phrase 'أَقَامَ أَبُويُوسُفَ الالي اَكْثَرَ الْحَوْل مَقَام كُلِهِ تَيْسِيرًا' clearly indicates Abu Yusuf's position and the reason: taysir (ease).
2
Understand each imam's evidence (dalil)
Imam Muhammad relies on the general principle that a right of Allah (ḥaqq Allāh) is not removed without complete impediment. Insanity is a partial impediment; as long as any part of the year remained with sanity, the obligation stands. Abu Yusuf argues that requiring full coverage leads to unnecessary hardship when the insanity covers the vast majority; the spirit of the law (maqāṣid) favors ease. His dalil is istiḥsān based on the Quranic principle 'yurīdu Llāhu bikumu l-yusra' (2:185).
Both are valid within the Hanafi school. The difference is one of taṭbīq (application) of the underlying maṣlaḥah.
3
Apply to a scenario
Suppose a person is insane for 11 months of the zakaah year and recovers the 12th month. Under Imam Muhammad, since the insanity did not cover the full year, zakat is still wajib; the person must pay zakat for that year. Under Abu Yusuf, since most of the year (11/12) was covered, the obligation drops; no zakat is due.
The thamarah al-khilaf is clear: the presence or absence of a financial obligation.
4
Determine the operative ruling (muftā bihi)
The text does not give a tarjīḥ, but later Hanafi authorities (e.g., Ibn ʿĀbidīn, Al-Haskafī) often adopt Abū Yūsuf's view as the operative fatwa because it aligns with the principle of ease and is more practical for the afflicted.
Students should know both opinions but be aware of which is more likely used in fatwa today.
5
Reflect on the significance of the ikhtilāf
This dispute shows that even within a single school, differences arise from the application of overarching principles (usul) to specific cases. Understanding the reasons for the difference strengthens one's ability to evaluate later tarjīḥ.
It also highlights the role of taysīr as a harmonizing principle in Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَاَمَّا الْجُنُونُ فَإِنَّهُ يُوجِبُ الْحَجْرَ عَنِ الْأَقْوَال وَيَسْقُطُ بِهِ مَا كَانَ ضَرَرًا يَحْتَمِلُ السُّقُوطَ وَإِذَا امْتَدَ فَصَارَ لُزُوْمُ الْأَدَاءِ يُؤَدِئُ إلَى الْحَرَج فَيَبْطُلُ الْقَوْلُ بِالْآدَاءِ وَيَنْعَدِمُ الْوُجُوْب أَيْضًا لِإِنْعِدَامِهِ وَحَدُ الْإِمْتِدَادِ فِي الصَّوْمِ أَن يَسْتَوْعِبَ الشَّهْرَ وَفي الصَّلَواتِ أَن يَزِيدَ عَلَى يَوْمٍ وَلَيْلَةٍ وفِي الزَّكَوةِ أَنْ يَسْتَوْعِبَ الْحَوْلَ عِنْدَ مُحَمَّدِ اللهِ وَأَقَامَ أَبُويُوسُفَ الالي اَكْثَرَ الْحَوْل مَقَام كُلِهِ تَيْسِيرًا وَمَا كَانَ حَسَنًا لَا يَحْتَمِلُ الْغَيْرَ أَوْ قَبِيْحًا لَا يَحْتَمِلُ الْعَفْوَ فَثَابِتٌ فِى حَقَّهِ حَتَّى يَثْبِتَ أَيْمَانُهُ وَرِدَّتُهُ تَبْعَا لَا بَوَيْهِ
As for insanity, it necessitates restriction from verbal [acts] and by it falls whatever is a harm that admits fall. And when it becomes prolonged, the necessity of performance leads to hardship, so the statement of performance becomes void and the obligation also ceases because of its ceasing. The limit of prolongation in fasting is that it encompasses the month, in prayers that it exceeds a day and night, and in zakat that it encompasses the year according to Muhammad. Abu Yusuf made most of the year stand in place of the entire year for ease. And what is good that does not admit otherwise or evil that does not admit pardon is established in his right, so that his faith and apostasy are established as a consequence, not by itself.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling on insanity (junūn) as an impediment to legal capacity. Insanity causes ḥajr (restriction) concerning verbal acts (aqwāl), meaning the insane person's statements (like divorce, manumission, gift) are ineffective. However, harmful acts that admit fall—such as acts that can lapse due to valid excuses—are removed by insanity. When insanity is prolonged (imtidād), insisting on performance of obligations leads to hardship (ḥaraj), so the obligation of performance drops, and the underlying obligation itself ceases. The text specifies the extent of prolongation: for fasting, insanity must cover the entire month; for prayers, more than a day and night; for zakat, the entire year according to Imam Muhammad, while Abu Yusuf considers most of the year sufficient for ease. Acts that are purely good (like belief) or purely evil (like apostasy) remain established for the insane person, so his faith or apostasy (if he manifested it) is considered effective, but only as a consequence of his parents' status (if he was a child following them), not independently. This ruling balances the removal of hardship while preserving core religious status.

Key Terms
الْحَجْر (al-ḥajr): Legal restriction preventing the validity of verbal acts of an insane person.
الْحَوْل (al-ḥawl): The full lunar year used as the zakaah period.
تَيْسِير (taysīr): The principle of facilitation; making the law easier for people in hardship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the reason Abu Yusuf suffices with most of the year for dropping zakat?
A) Because the year is long
B) For ease (taysīr)
C) Because it is the opinion of the majority
D) Because the insane person cannot own property

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has two positions on the zakat of an insane person: full year (Muhammad) or most of the year (Abu Yusuf); the latter is often favored for ease.

Understanding 'Atah (Idiocy) After Puberty: Hanafi Usul Definition and Rulings
Defining 'atah and comparing to childhood

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'atah (idiocy) according to Hanafi usul, apply its classification to issues of liability and validity of actions, and distinguish it from childhood and madness.

Core Idea
'Atah (idiocy) after puberty removes liability (uhdah) but not the validity of actions, just like a discerning child.

This passage from Al-Husami introduces the legal category of 'atah (idiocy) as it appears after puberty. You will learn how Hanafi usul classifies this state, what rulings it affects, and how it differs from childhood and madness. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying liability and compensation rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core definition
The passage states that 'atah is like a discerning child in all rulings. Identify the two key effects: it does not prevent validity of speech/action, but it prevents uhdah (liability).
Note the parallel: 'like a child with intellect'.
2
Understand the scope of uhdah
The passage clarifies that compensation (daman) for property destruction is not considered uhdah; it is a separate obligation for recompense. Therefore, even an idiot must pay for damage.
Daman is prescribed 'jabran' (as recompense), not as a penal liability.
3
Determine the effect on legal capacity
The khitab (direct address of commands) is removed from the idiot, he is placed under guardianship, and he cannot be a guardian over others.
This mirrors the ruling for a discerning child.
4
Distinguish 'atah from madness (junun)
Madness is unbounded (no fixed end), so when a madman's wife converts to Islam, his parents are immediately offered Islam. Childhood is bounded (ends at puberty), so the offer can be delayed.
The boundedness affects the timeline of the ruling.
5
Apply to an intelligent idiot
The passage concludes that an intelligent child and an intelligent idiot are treated identically in rulings.
This emphasizes the analogy between the two categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا الْعَتَهُ بَعْدَ البُلُوغ فَمِثْلُ الصَّبَامَعَ الْعَقْلِ فِي كُلِّ الْأَحْكَام حَتَّى أَنَّهُ لَا يَمْنَعُ صِحةَ الْقَوْلِ وَالْفَعْلِ لكِنَّهُ يَمْنَعُ الْعُهْدَةَ وَأَمَّا ضَمَانُ مَا يُسْتَهْلَكَ مِنْ الْأَمْوَالِ فَلَيْسَ بِعُهْدَةٍ لأَنَّهُ شُرِعَ جَبْرًا وَكَوْنُهُ صَبَيًا مَعْذُوْرًا أَوْ مَعْتُوْهًا لَا يُنَافِيْ عِصْمَةَ الْمَحْلِ وَيُوْضَعْ عَنْهُ الْخِطَابَ كَمَا يُوْضِعُ عَنِ الصَّبِي وَيُوَلَّى عَلَيْهِ وَلَا يَلِيْ هُوَ عَلَى غَيْرِهِ وَإِنَّمَا يَفْتَرِقُ الْجُنُونَ وَالغْرُ فِى أَنَّ هَذَا الْعَارِضَ غَيْرُ مَحْدُوْدٍ فَقِيْلَ إِذَا أَسْلَمَتْ اِمْرَأَتُهُ عُرضَ عَلَى أَبِيهِ وَأَمِهِ الْإِسْلَامُ وَلَا يُؤَخَرُ وَالصَّبَا مَحْدُودٌ فَوَجَبَ تَا خِيْرُهُ وَأَمَّا الصَّبِيُّ الْعَاقِلُ وَالْمَعْتُوهُ الْعَاقِلُ فَلَا يَفْتَرِقَانِ
As for idiocy ('atah) after puberty, it is like childhood with intellect in all rulings, to the extent that it does not prevent the validity of speech and action, but it prevents liability (uhdah). As for compensation (daman) for what is destroyed of wealth, that is not [a matter of] uhdah because it is prescribed as recompense. His being an excused child or an idiot does not contradict the inviolability of the property. And the address (khitab) is removed from him just as it is removed from the child, and he is placed under guardianship and does not have guardianship over others. The difference between madness and childhood is that this impediment (madness) is unbounded: thus if his wife embraces Islam, his parents are presented with Islam and it is not delayed. In contrast, childhood is bounded, so delaying it is obligatory. As for the intelligent child (sabi 'aqil) and the intelligent idiot (ma'tuh 'aqil), they are not different.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage introduces 'atah (idiocy) occurring after puberty, which resembles a discerning child in rulings. It does not invalidate speech or actions (e.g., contracts may be valid) but removes liability (uhdah) for penalties. However, compensation for destroyed property is not considered uhdah but rather a restorative payment, so it is still due even from a child or idiot, because property rights remain inviolable. The text clarifies that the khitab (direct address of legal commands) is lifted from the idiot, and he is under guardianship, not a guardian over others. A key distinction between madness and childhood: madness is unbounded (can last indefinitely), so when a madman's wife embraces Islam, his parents are immediately offered Islam (to avoid prolonged separation). Childhood is bounded (ends at puberty), so the offer can be delayed until the child reaches maturity. Finally, the intelligent child and intelligent idiot are treated alike in rulings.

Key Terms
عتة (atah): Idiocy or mental deficiency that persists after puberty, removing liability (uhdah) but not the validity of utterances and actions.
عهدة (uhdah): Legal liability for an act, especially penal responsibility.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the difference between madness (junun) and childhood (sughr) regarding the offering of Islam when the insane person's wife converts?
A) For madness, Islam is offered immediately; for childhood, it can be delayed.
B) For childhood, Islam is offered immediately; for madness, it is delayed.
C) Both are offered immediately.
D) Both are delayed until recovery or puberty.

Key Takeaway
Idiocy after puberty parallels a discerning child: actions are valid, penal liability is removed, but property compensation remains due; the key distinction from madness is the unbounded duration of idiocy.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
