ترجمہ :۔ اور بہر حال حیض اور نفاس پس یہ دونوں کسی بھی طرح اہلیت کو معدوم نہیں کرتے ہیں لیکن ان دونوں سے طہارت شرط ہے روزہ اور نماز کی ادا کے جواز کے لیے، پس ان دونوں کی وجہ سے ادا فوت ہو جائے گی ، اور نماز کی قضا میں حرج ہے ، نماز کے تضاعف (بڑھ جانے ) کی وجہ سے ، تو ان دونوں کی وجہ سے نماز کا اصل وجوب ساقط ہو جائے گا ، اور روزہ کی قضا میں کوئی حرج نہیں ہے تو روزہ کا اصل وجوب ساقط نہ ہو گا۔
اور بہر حال موت پس وہ خالص عجز ہے، اس کی وجہ سے تمام احکام ساقط ہو جائیں گے جو تکلیف کے قبیل سے ہیں، تکلیف کی غرض کے فوت ہونے کی وجہ سے، اور وہ غرض اختیار سے ادا کرنا ہے، اور اسی وجہ سے ہم نے کہا کہ میت سے زکوۃ اور عبادات کی تمام صورتیں باطل ہو جائیں گی ، اور اس پر گناہ باقی رہے گا۔ اور وہ حکم جو میت پر مشروع ہوتا ہے دوسرے کی حاجت کی وجہ سے، اگر وہ ایسا حق ہے جو عین کے ساتھ متعلق ہے، تو وہ حق باقی رہے گا عین کی بقاء کے ساتھ ، اس لئے کہ اس میں میت کا فعل مقصود نہیں ہے اور اگر وہ دین ہے تو وہ محض ذمہ کی وجہ سے باقی نہیں رہے گا، یہانتک کہ مل جائے ذمہ کے ساتھ مال یا وہ چیز جس سے ذمے مؤکد ہو جاتے ہیں، اور وہ کفیل کا ذمہ ہے، اور اسی وجہ سے امام ابوحنیفہ عالیہ نے فرمایا کہ میت کی طرف سے دین کا کفیل ہونا صحیح نہیں ہے جب کہ میت نے مال یا کفیل نہ چھوڑا ہو، گویا کہ دین اس سے ساقط ہے، برخلاف عبد مجور کے جو دین کا اقرار کرے ، پھر اس کی طرف سے کوئی شخص کفیل ہو جائے تو صحیح ہے، اس لئے کہ کفیل کا ذمہ اس کے حق میں کامل ہے، اور اس کے ذمہ کے ساتھ مولی کے حق میں مالیت ملادی جاتی ہے، اور اگر وہ حکم میت پر صلہ کے طور پر مشروع ہو تو باطل ہو جائے گا مگر یہ کہ اس کی وصیت کر دی جائے تو وہ ثلث مال سے صحیح ہو گا۔
تشریح:- نواں عارض حیض اور دسواں عارض نفاس ہے، حیض و نفاس کسی بھی طرح اہلیت کو تو ختم نہیں کرتے لیکن نماز اور روزہ کی ادائیگی کے لئے حیض و نفاس سے طہارت شرط ہے، لہذا حیض و نفاس کے ساتھ نماز اور روزہ کی ادا ئیگی نہیں ہوسکتی ، پس حیض و نفاس والی عورت نہ نماز پڑھے گی ، نہ روزہ رکھے گی۔ پھر اس کی قضا ہوگی یا نہیں؟ تو فرمایا کہ روزوں کی قضا ہے اور نماز کی قضا نہیں ہے ، کیونکہ نمازیں بہت زیادہ ہو جائے گی اس کی قضا دشوار ہے، تو شریعت نے نماز کا اصل وجوب ہی ساقط کر دیا، اور روزوں کی قضا دشوار نہیں اس لئے روزہ کا اصل وجوب باقی رکھا، البتہ ادائیگی سے منع کر دیا، پاک ہونے کے بعد پھر اس کی قضا کر لے۔ واما الموت : - بارھواں عارض موت ہے، موت سے بھی بندہ عاج محض ہو جاتا ہے، لہذا موت کی وجہ سے وہ تمام احکام ساقط ہو جاتے ہیں جن کا زندگی میں بندہ مکلف ہوتا ہے، کیونکہ موت سے مکلف ہونے کی غرض ہی فوت ہو جاتی ہے، اور وہ غرض ہے اپنے اختیار سے ادا کرنا ، اور میت اختیار ندا در ہے، لہذا نماز، روزہ، زکوۃ وغیرہ دنیوی اعتبار سے اس پر ساقط شمار ہوں گے، لہذا اس کے ترکہ سے زکوۃ کی ادائیگی واجب نہ ہوگی ، البتہ اخروی اعتبار سے اس پر گناہ باقی رہے گا۔ جاننا چاہیے کہ احکام دو طرح کے ہیں (۱) احکام دنیوی (۲) احکام اخروی ، پھر احکام دنیوی چار قسم کے ہیں (۱) وہ احکام جو تکلیف کے قبیل سے ہیں، اس کا حکم اوپر بیان ہو گیا کہ تکلیف کے قبیل کے سب احکام میت سے ساقط ہو جاتے ہیں۔
وما شرع عليه لحاجة غيره : - احکام دنیوی کی دوسری قسم کو بیان کر رہے ہیں ، کہ وہ احکام جو میت پر دوسروں کے لیے مشروع ہوتے ہیں، ان کی تین قسمیں ہیں (۱) پہلی قسم یہ ہے کہ وہ میت پر ایسا حق ہو جو عین کے ساتھ متعلق ہو، تو وہ حق مین کے باقی رہنے کی وجہ سے موت کے بعد بھی باقی رہے گا جیسے شئی مرہون ، یا مبیع جو مشتری کو سپرد نہ کی ہو، یاور بیعت وغیرہ یعنی اگر میت کے پاس کسی نے کوئی چیز رہن رکھی ہے یا میت نے کوئی چیز بیچی ہے اور ابھی مبیع مشتری کو سپر دنہیں کی ہے، یا میت کے پاس کوئی چیز غصب کی ہوئی ہے، یاکسی کی امانت ہے تو ان تمام چیزوں میں صاحب حق کا حق عین شی سے متعلق رہے گا، لہذا یہ چیزیں میت کے ترکہ یا وصیت میں شامل نہ ہوں گی، دلیل یہ ہے کہ حقوق العباد میں عین شی مقصود ہوتی ہے فعل مقصود نہیں ہوتا، تو جب تک شئی موجود ہے، حق باقی رہے گا بر خلاف حقوق اللہ کے کہ ان میں فعل مقصود ہوتا ہے، یعنی اللہ بندہ کا فعل دیکھتا ہے کہ بندہ اپنے اختیار سے ادا کرتا ہے یا نہیں۔ (۲) دوسری قسم یہ ہے کہ وہ حق جو دوسرے کی ضرورت کی وجہ سے مشروع ہے، اگر وہ دین وقرض کے قبیل سے ہے جو واجب فی الذمہ ہوتا ہے جیسے دیون واجبه مثلا من بیچ ، یا مہر یا قرض وغیرہ تو یہ حق محض میت کے ذمہ کی وجہ سے باقی نہ رہے گا، بلکہ میت کے ذمہ کے ساتھ مال ملا ہو یعنی میت نے مال چھوڑا ہو، یا وہ چیز ملی ہو جس سے ذمہ مضبوط ہوتا ہے یعنی میت نے اپنا کفیل چھوڑا ہو، تو ان صورتوں میں دین باقی رہے گا، پہلی صورت میں مال سے دین وصول کیا جائے گا، اور دوسری صورت میں کفیل سے دین کا مطالبہ کیا جائے گا، اور اگر نہ مال چھوڑا ہونہ کفیل ہوتو دینوی اعتبار سے دین ساقط ہو جائے گا، لہذامیت کی اولاد سے دین کا مطالبہ نہیں کیا جائے گا، ہاں اخروی اعتبار سے میت پر مؤاخذہ ہوگا۔
چونکہ محض میت کے ذمہ کی وجہ سے دین باقی نہیں رہتا ہے، اس لئے امام ابو حنیفہ علیہ فرماتے ہیں کہ میت نے اگر مال نہیں چھوڑا اور نہ کفیل چھوڑا تو میت کی طرف سے دین کا کفیل بننا صحیح نہیں ہے، کیونکہ دینوی اعتبار سے اس کا
Translation: And as for menstruation and postpartum bleeding, these two do not nullify legal capacity in any way, but purification from them is a condition for the validity of performing prayer and fasting. So due to these two, performance is missed. And there is difficulty in making up prayers because of the multiplicity of prayers. Therefore, due to these two, the original obligation of prayer is dropped. And there is no difficulty in making up fasts, so the original obligation of fasting is not dropped. And as for death, it is pure incapacity. Due to it, all rulings that are of the category of obligation (taklif) are dropped, because the purpose of obligation is lost—and that purpose is to perform with choice. For this reason we said that from the deceased, zakat and all forms of worship become void, and sin remains upon him. And a ruling that is legislated for the deceased due to the need of others: if it is a right that is attached to a specific object, then that right remains with the survival of the object, because the act of the deceased is not intended therein. And if it is a debt, then it does not remain merely due to the liability of the deceased, unless property is combined with the liability, or something that strengthens the liability—and that is the guarantee of a surety. For this reason Imam Abu Hanifa (may Allah elevate him) said that being a guarantor for the debt of a deceased is not valid when the deceased left neither property nor a surety, as if the debt is dropped from him. Unlike a mukatab slave who acknowledges a debt, and then someone becomes a guarantor for him—it is valid, because the surety's liability is complete in his regard, and monetary value is added to his liability in relation to the master. And if the ruling is legislated for the deceased as a gift (silah), it becomes void unless he made a will about it, then it is valid from one-third of the estate. Explanation: The ninth impediment is menstruation and the tenth is postpartum bleeding. Menstruation and postpartum bleeding do not eliminate legal capacity at all, but purification from them is a condition for performing prayer and fasting. Therefore, with menstruation and postpartum bleeding, prayer and fasting cannot be performed. So a woman in menstruation or postpartum bleeding will neither pray nor fast. Then, is she required to make them up? It is said that fasts are to be made up, but prayers are not, because prayers become numerous and their make-up is difficult, so the Lawgiver dropped the original obligation of prayer. And make-up of fasts is not difficult, so the original obligation of fasting remains; however, performance is prohibited. After purification, she makes them up. As for death: the twelfth impediment is death. By death a person becomes completely incapable. Therefore, due to death, all rulings that a person was obligated with in life are dropped, because the purpose of obligation is lost—and that purpose is to perform with choice, and the deceased has no choice. So prayer, fasting, zakat, etc., in worldly terms are considered dropped from him. Thus, payment of zakat from his estate is not obligatory. However, in the hereafter, sin remains upon him. It should be known that rulings are of two types: (1) worldly rulings, (2) hereafter rulings. Then worldly rulings are of four types: (1) Those rulings that are of the category of obligation (taklif); their ruling was stated above: all rulings of the category of obligation are dropped from the deceased. 'And what is legislated for him due to the need of others'—the second type of worldly rulings is being explained: those rulings that are legislated for the deceased for the sake of others are of three types: (1) The first type is that there is a right on the deceased that is attached to a specific object; then that right remains after death due to the survival of the object, such as a mortgaged object, or a sold object that has not been delivered to the buyer, or an object of usurpation, etc. That is, if someone has pledged something with the deceased, or the deceased sold something and has not yet delivered it to the buyer, or the deceased has usurped something, or someone's trust is with him, then in all these cases the right of the owner remains attached to the specific object. Therefore, these items will not be included in the deceased's estate or will. The proof is that in rights of people, the specific object is intended, not the act. So as long as the object exists, the right remains, unlike the rights of Allah, in which the act is intended—i.e., Allah looks at whether the servant performs the act with his own choice or not. (2) The second type is that right which is legislated due to the need of another; if it is of the category of debt or loan that is obligatory upon the liability, such as obligatory debts, e.g., price of a sale, dower, loan, etc., then this right does not remain merely due to the liability of the deceased. Rather, if property is combined with the deceased's liability—i.e., the deceased left property—or something that strengthens the liability exists—i.e., the deceased left a surety—then in these cases the debt remains. In the first case, the debt will be collected from the property; in the second case, it will be demanded from the surety. And if he left neither property nor a surety, then in worldly terms the debt is dropped. Therefore, the debt will not be demanded from the deceased's children. Yes, in the hereafter, the deceased will be held accountable. Since the debt does not remain merely due to the liability of the deceased, Imam Abu Hanifa (may Allah be pleased with him) says: If the deceased left no property and no surety, then becoming a guarantor for the debt of the deceased is not valid, because in worldly terms it is
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage is from a commentary on Al-Husami, explaining the effects of certain impediments (awārid) on legal capacity and obligations. The author discusses two main impediments: menstruation/postpartum bleeding (hayd and nifas), and death (mawt). For hayd and nifas, the key point is that they do not nullify legal capacity itself but they prevent the performance of prayer and fasting because purity is a condition. The ruling differs for prayer and fasting: prayers are not made up due to the difficulty of making up many prayers (since a woman may miss several days), so the original obligation is dropped; fasting, however, is made up because it is not overly burdensome. For death, it is pure incapacity, so all obligations (taklifi rulings) are dropped in this world—meaning the deceased is no longer required to pray, fast, pay zakat, etc., from his estate. However, sin remains in the Hereafter if the obligations were missed without valid reason. The author then categorizes rulings legislated for the deceased due to the need of others into three types: (1) Rights attached to a specific object (e.g., pledged property, sold goods not delivered, usurped items, trusts) remain because the object itself is owed, not the deceased's act. (2) Debts owed by the deceased (e.g., sale price, dower, loans) remain only if the deceased left property or a surety; otherwise, in worldly terms the debt is dropped (though the deceased is still accountable in the Hereafter). (3) Acts of worship that are purely rewards (silah) like voluntary charity, if not willed, are void; but if willed, they are executed from one-third of the estate. A significant ruling from Imam Abu Hanifa is that guaranteeing a debt of a deceased person is invalid if the deceased left no property and no surety, because the debt is effectively extinguished in this world—the guarantor would be guaranteeing something that no longer exists. This is contrasted with a similar situation for a living person (mukātab slave) where guaranteeing a debt is valid.

Key Terms
عارض (āriḍ): An impediment that temporarily or permanently affects legal capacity
تکلیف (taklīf): Legal obligation that requires a capable person to perform or refrain with choice
قضاء (qaḍāʾ): Compensatory performance of an missed obligatory act after its time
ذمہ (dhimma): Legal liability or capacity of a person to owe obligations
کفیل (kafīl): A surety who guarantees the debt of another

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why are missed prayers not required to be made up by a woman after her menstruation ends, whereas missed fasts must be made up?
A) Because prayers are more important than fasts, so they are dropped to lighten burden.
B) Because the number of missed prayers would be excessive and making them up would be burdensome, whereas fasts are manageable.
C) Because prayers require physical purity which cannot be achieved during menstruation, while fasting does not.
D) Because the original obligation of prayer is voided by menstruation, while for fasting only performance is delayed.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between temporary impediments that suspend performance but leave the obligation intact (with case-by-case hardship analysis) and permanent impediments that terminate all taklīfī obligations while preserving certain rights of others.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
8 min
Obligations of Taklif Drop at Death; Rights in Rem Survive
Classification of worldly ahkam after death and survival of rights in rem

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that obligations requiring personal choice (taklif) drop at death, and distinguish which rights survive as rights in rem (haqq 'ayni) from those that do not.

Core Idea
Death ends the capacity for voluntary action, so all obligations of taklif drop – but rights attached to physical objects survive regardless.

This lesson explores a foundational usul principle: death removes the subject's capacity for choice-based performance, so all obligations that depend on personal action fall away. However, not all legal consequences vanish – rights that are tied to a specific object (haqq 'ayni) persist because they are independent of the deceased's action. We will trace the reasoning behind this classification and see how it determines the status of acts of worship, mortgages, and trusts after death.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The passage states that all rulings requiring taklif (obligation with choice) drop at death. Determine the rationale: death removes the very purpose of obligation, which is voluntary performance.
This is a rational (ma'qul) rather than a textual (naqli) proof.
2
Classify the two types of rulings
Rulings are divided into worldly (dunyawi) and afterlife (ukhrawi). Only worldly rulings are affected in terms of legal demands. Understand that afterlife accountability (sin) may remain even when worldly obligations drop.
This distinction helps reconcile conflicting intuitions about whether debts 'expire'.
3
Analyze the first subtype: rights in rem
For rights attached to a specific thing (haqq 'ayni), such as mortgages, sold goods not delivered, usurped property, or trusts, the right survives death because it is tied to the object, not to the deceased's action. These items are excluded from the estate and inherited separately.
Proof: In huquq al-ibad, the object (ayn) is intended, not the act (fi'l).
4
Compare with second subtype: debts
Debts (dayn) are a liability on the dhimma. According to Abu Hanifa, they do not persist by mere dhimma; they require property or a surety to survive. If neither exists, the debt drops in this world (though afterlife sin remains).
This introduces the dispute with Sahibayn and Shafi'i, which will be detailed in Lesson 2.
5
Apply to a concrete scenario
Given a deceased with a mortgaged item and an unpaid zakah, determine the status of each: the mortgaged property is reclaimed by the lender (haqq 'ayni survives), while the zakah obligation drops (it is a haqq Allah dependent on personal action).
This illustrates the practical outcome of the classification.

وہ تمام احکام ساقط ہو جاتے ہیں جن کا زندگی میں بندہ مکلف ہوتا ہے، کیونکہ موت سے مکلف ہونے کی غرض ہی فوت ہو جاتی ہے، اور وہ غرض ہے اپنے اختیار سے ادا کرنا ، اور میت اختیار ندا در ہے، لہذا نماز، روزہ، زکوۃ وغیرہ دنیوی اعتبار سے اس پر ساقط شمار ہوں گے، لہذا اس کے ترکہ سے زکوۃ کی ادائیگی واجب نہ ہوگی ، البتہ اخروی اعتبار سے اس پر گناہ باقی رہے گا۔ جاننا چاہیے کہ احکام دو طرح کے ہیں (۱) احکام دنیوی (۲) احکام اخروی ، پھر احکام دنیوی چار قسم کے ہیں (۱) وہ احکام جو تکلیف کے قبیل سے ہیں، اس کا حکم اوپر بیان ہو گیا کہ تکلیف کے قبیل کے سب احکام میت سے ساقط ہو جاتے ہیں۔
وما شرع عليه لحاجة غيره : - احکام دنیوی کی دوسری قسم کو بیان کر رہے ہیں ، کہ وہ احکام جو میت پر دوسروں کے لیے مشروع ہوتے ہیں، ان کی تین قسمیں ہیں (۱) پہلی قسم یہ ہے کہ وہ میت پر ایسا حق ہو جو عین کے ساتھ متعلق ہو، تو وہ حق مین کے باقی رہنے کی وجہ سے موت کے بعد بھی باقی رہے گا جیسے شئی مرہون ، یا مبیع جو مشتری کو سپرد نہ کی ہو، یاور بیعت وغیرہ یعنی اگر میت کے پاس کسی نے کوئی چیز رہن رکھی ہے یا میت نے کوئی چیز بیچی ہے اور ابھی مبیع مشتری کو سپر دنہیں کی ہے، یا میت کے پاس کوئی چیز غصب کی ہوئی ہے، یاکسی کی امانت ہے تو ان تمام چیزوں میں صاحب حق کا حق عین شی سے متعلق رہے گا، لہذا یہ چیزیں میت کے ترکہ یا وصیت میں شامل نہ ہوں گی، دلیل یہ ہے کہ حقوق العباد میں عین شی مقصود ہوتی ہے فعل مقصود نہیں ہوتا، تو جب تک شئی موجود ہے، حق باقی رہے گا بر خلاف حقوق اللہ کے کہ ان میں فعل مقصود ہوتا ہے، یعنی اللہ بندہ کا فعل دیکھتا ہے کہ بندہ اپنے اختیار سے ادا کرتا ہے یا نہیں۔ (۲) دوسری قسم یہ ہے کہ وہ حق جو دوسرے کی ضرورت کی وجہ سے مشروع ہے، اگر وہ دین وقرض کے قبیل سے ہے جو واجب فی الذمہ ہوتا ہے جیسے دیون واجبه مثلا من بیچ ، یا مہر یا قرض وغیرہ تو یہ حق محض میت کے ذمہ کی وجہ سے باقی نہ رہے گا، بلکہ میت کے ذمہ کے ساتھ مال ملا ہو یعنی میت نے مال چھوڑا ہو، یا وہ چیز ملی ہو جس سے ذمہ مضبوط ہوتا ہے یعنی میت نے اپنا کفیل چھوڑا ہو، تو ان صورتوں میں دین باقی رہے گا، پہلی صورت میں مال سے دین وصول کیا جائے گا، اور دوسری صورت میں کفیل سے دین کا مطالبہ کیا جائے گا، اور اگر نہ مال چھوڑا ہونہ کفیل ہوتو دینوی اعتبار سے دین ساقط ہو جائے گا، لہذامیت کی اولاد سے دین کا مطالبہ نہیں کیا جائے گا، ہاں اخروی اعتبار سے میت پر مؤاخذہ ہوگا۔
چونکہ محض میت کے ذمہ کی وجہ سے دین باقی نہیں رہتا ہے، اس لئے امام ابو حنیفہ علیہ فرماتے ہیں کہ میت نے اگر مال نہیں چھوڑا اور نہ کفیل چھوڑا تو میت کی طرف سے دین کا کفیل بننا صحیح نہیں ہے، کیونکہ دینوی اعتبار سے اس کا دین ساقط ہو جاتا ہے، البتہ بطور احسان کے کوئی اس کی طرف سے قرض ادا کر دے تو بالا تفاق صحیح ہے، صاحبین اور امام شافعی فرماتے ہیں کہ میت کی طرف سے دین کا کفیل بنا صحیح ہے، چاہے اس نے مال و کفیل نہ چھوڑا ہو، کیونکہ موت انسان کو قرض سے بری نہیں کرتی ، اس لئے بطور تبرع کے قرض کی ادائیگی میت کی طرف سے درست ہے۔ بخلاف العبد المحجور: ایک اعتراض کا جواب ہے، جس کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ ذمہ کا ضعیف ہونا میت اور عبد مجور دونوں میں برابر ہے، تو پھر میت کی طرف سے کفالت صحیح نہیں ، اور عبد مجور کی طرف سے کفیل بنا صحیح ہے ایسا کیوں؟
اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ عبد مجور اگر کسی کے دین کا اقرار کرے تو اس کی طرف سے کفیل ہونا صحیح ہے، اس لئے کہ عبد مجور کے عاقل بالغ اور مکلف ہونے کی وجہ سے اس کا ذمہ کامل ہے، اور اگر آقا دین کی تصدیق کرے یا اس کو آزاد کرے تو فی الحال مطالبہ بھی صحیح ہے تو کفالت بھی صحیح ہے، کیوں کہ کفالت کا مدار مطالبہ کی صحت پر ہے یعنی جس سے مطالبہ صحیح ہے اس کی کفالت صحیح ہے۔ اور میت میں چونکہ مطالبہ درست نہیں تو کفالت بھی درست نہیں ہے ، اور اگر مولی دین کی تصدیق نہ کرے یا آزاد نہ کرے تو عبد مجور سے فی الحال اگرچہ مطالبہ درست نہیں ہے ، مگر اس سے مطالبہ ممکن ہے ، جب آقا آزاد کرے گا تو مطالبہ درست ہوگا، لہذا کفالت صحیح ہے۔
.
وانما ضمت اليه المالية :- ایک سوال مقدر کا جواب ہے، کہ جب عبد مجور کا ذمہ کامل ہے تو آقا کے حق میں اس کے ساتھ مالیت رقبہ کو کیوں ملایا گیا ہے، یعنی آقا کے حق میں اس کو مالیت کیوں مانا گیا، کہ یہ آقا کا مال ہے۔ اس کا جواب دے رہے ہیں کہ عبد مجور کا ذمہ خود اس کے حق میں تو کامل ہے کیوں کہ وہ عاقل بالغ ہے ، مگر مولی کے حق میں اس کا ذمہ ناقص ہے ، اور مولی کے حق میں ناقص اس لئے مانا گیا تا کہ مولی نقصان سے بچ جائے ، کیوں کہ اگر مولی کے حق اس کا ذمہ کامل مانا گیا تو غلام سے فی الحال مطالبہ درست ہوگا تو غلام آزاد ہو جائے گا ، اور اس میں مولی کا نقصان ہے، مولی کو نقصان سے بچانے کے لئے مولی کے حق میں ذمہ کے ساتھ مالیت رقبہ کو ملایا گیا، کہ یہ مولی کا رقبہ ہے، لہذا فی الحال غلام سے مطالبہ درست نہ رہا، اور نہ مولی کی مرضی کے بغیر اس کو فروخت کرنا درست رہا۔ (۳) تیسری قسم وہ احکام جو میت پر بطور صلہ واحسان کے مشروع ہیں جیسے محارم کا نفقہ، صدقۂ فطر وغیرہ تو یہ احکام بھی موت سے باطل ہو جائیں گے ، لہذا محارم کا نفقہ میت کے ذمہ گذشتہ ایام کا ہو، یا آئندہ سب ساقط ہو جائے گا، محارم کو یہ حق نہیں ہے کہ میراث سے وصول کریں۔ البتہ اگر میت نے صلہ وتبرع کی وصیت کی ہو تو تہائی مال سے اس کی وصیت نافذ کر دی جائے گی۔
All rulings for which a living person is obligated are dropped, because death removes the purpose of being obligated, which is performing with choice, and the deceased has no choice. Therefore, prayer, fasting, zakah, etc., are considered dropped from a worldly perspective, so paying zakah from his estate is not obligatory; however, from an afterlife perspective, sin remains. Know that rulings are of two types: (1) worldly rulings, (2) afterlife rulings. Then worldly rulings are four types: (1) rulings that are of the category of obligation; their ruling was stated above: all obligations of this category are dropped from the deceased. (2) Those legislated for the need of others - this explains the second type of worldly rulings: rulings legislated for the sake of others upon the deceased. They are three kinds: (1) A right that is attached to a specific thing, such that that right remains after death due to the continued existence of the thing, like a mortgaged item, or a sold item not yet delivered to the buyer, or a usurped item, or a trust. In all these, the right-holder's right is attached to the specific thing, so these items are not included in the deceased's estate or will. The proof is that in rights of people, the specific thing is intended, not the action. So as long as the thing exists, the right continues, contrary to rights of Allah where the action is intended. (2) [Second kind:] A right legislated due to another's need; if it is of the type of debt that is obligatory upon the dhimma, like compulsory debts, e.g., sale price, dower, loan, etc., then this right does not remain merely due to the deceased's dhimma; rather, if property is left by the deceased or something strengthening the dhimma, i.e., a surety, then the debt remains. In the first case, the debt is collected from the property; in the second, demand is made from the surety. If neither property nor surety is left, then from a worldly perspective the debt is dropped, so the debt cannot be demanded from the deceased's children, though from an afterlife perspective the deceased will be held accountable. Since mere dhimma of the deceased does not keep the debt alive, Imam Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) says: if the deceased left no property or surety, then becoming surety for the deceased's debt is not valid, because from a worldly perspective his debt is dropped. However, if someone voluntarily pays the debt on his behalf, it is valid by consensus. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and Imam al-Shafi'i say: becoming surety for the deceased's debt is valid even if he left no property or surety, because death does not free one from debt; therefore, voluntary payment of the debt on behalf of the deceased is valid. Unlike the restricted slave (abd mahjur): this answers an objection, namely that weak dhimma is common to the deceased and the restricted slave, so why is suretyship for the deceased invalid, but for the restricted slave valid? The answer: if the restricted slave acknowledges a debt, becoming surety for him is valid because he is sane, adult, and obligated, so his dhimma is complete. If the master confirms the debt or frees him, demand becomes valid immediately, so suretyship is valid, because suretyship depends on the validity of demand: whoever can be validly demanded, suretyship for him is valid. Since demand is not valid for the deceased, suretyship is not valid. And if the master does not confirm or free him, demand is not immediately valid for the slave, but demand is possible; when the master frees, demand becomes valid, so suretyship is valid. (Objection answered:) "and why is financial worth attached to it?" - this answers a hypothetical question: when the restricted slave's dhimma is complete, why is financial worth of his person attached to it, i.e., why is he considered property of the master? The answer is that the slave's dhimma is complete regarding himself, but regarding the master it is deficient. It is considered deficient for the master to protect the master from loss, because if the dhimma were considered complete regarding the master, immediate demand from the slave would be valid, leading to the slave's freedom, harming the master. To prevent harm, the slave's financial worth as property is attached to the dhimma regarding the master. Therefore, immediate demand from the slave is not valid, nor is selling him without the master's consent. (3) The third kind: rulings legislated for the deceased as a reward and favor, like maintenance of relatives, sadaqat al-fitr, etc. These rulings become void by death, so maintenance for past or future days is dropped; relatives have no right to collect it from the inheritance. However, if the deceased made a bequest as a favor or gift, it will be executed from one-third of the estate.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Husami discusses a fundamental usul principle: how death affects different types of legal obligations (ahkam). The author begins by stating a general rule: obligations that require personal choice (taklif) are dropped by death, because the very purpose of obligation is voluntary performance. Thus, acts of worship like prayer, fasting, and zakah are not due from the deceased's estate in this world, though the deceased may bear sin in the afterlife for neglecting them. The passage then classifies worldly rulings into four categories, focusing on the second category: rulings legislated for the sake of others. These are further divided into three subtypes. 

The first subtype is rights attached to a specific thing (haqq al-ayn). These survive death because the right is tied to the object itself, not to the deceased's action. Examples include collateral, sold goods not yet delivered, usurped property, and trusts. These items are not part of the estate; the owner reclaims them.

The second subtype is debts (dayn) like sale price, dower, or loans. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, such debts do not persist merely due to the deceased's dhimma (legal liability); they require either assets left behind or a surety (kafil) provided by the deceased. If neither exists, the debt is dropped in this world (though the deceased remains answerable to Allah). The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and Imam al-Shafi'i disagree: they hold that death does not extinguish debts, so suretyship for the deceased is valid even without assets. The author explains the Hanafi position by noting that worldly demand is impossible against a corpse, so suretyship (which depends on valid demand) is invalid. However, voluntary payment (tabarru') is allowed by consensus.

The text then addresses a counterexample: why can a restricted slave (abd mahjur) have a valid surety if his dhimma is weak like that of a deceased? The slave, being sane and adult, has a complete dhimma in himself; his restriction is only regarding transactions without the master's permission. If he acknowledges a debt, demand against him is possible (e.g., if the master confirms or frees him), so suretyship is valid. For the deceased, demand is impossible in this world, so suretyship is invalid. Additionally, the slave's financial worth as property is attached to his dhimma regarding the master's rights, to prevent the master's loss (e.g., the slave being sold for debt and becoming free).

Finally, the third subtype includes obligations of goodwill like maintenance of relatives and sadaqat al-fitr. These are voided by death because they are not debts but rewards; no claim can be made from the inheritance unless the deceased made a bequest (within one-third of the estate).

Key Terms
حق عینی (haqq 'ayni): A right that attaches to a specific physical object, surviving the death of the obligor.
تکلیف (taklif): Legal obligation that requires the subject's choice and capacity (ahliyyat al-ada').
حق اللہ (haqq Allah): A right belonging to Allah, such as ritual acts; often based on the subject's action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do obligations like prayer and zakah drop at death?
A) Because they are rights of people (huquq al-ibad) and thus require action.
B) Because they are rights of Allah (huquq Allah) and require voluntary action, which is impossible after death.
C) Because the estate cannot afford to pay for them.
D) Because death wipes out all legal obligations without exception.

Key Takeaway
Obligations based on personal action drop at death, but rights attached to specific objects survive because they are independent of the deceased's act.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
10 min
Debts After Death: Property or Surety Required; Suretyship Dispute
Explanation of how debts (dayn) are affected by death and the dispute on suretyship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi position that a deceased's debt persists in this world only if property or surety is left, and evaluate the disagreement with the Sahibayn and Shafi'i regarding suretyship for the indebted deceased.

Core Idea
A dead man's debt is not a sure thing in this world – it needs collateral, either property or a living guarantor, to stay collectible.

This lesson drills into the second subtype of worldly rulings: debts (dayn). According to Imam Abu Hanifa, a debt owed by a deceased does not automatically remain enforceable. It survives only if the deceased left enough property to cover it or provided a surety (kafil). If neither exists, the debt is dropped from a worldly perspective, meaning the family and heirs cannot be forced to pay. However, the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and Imam al-Shafi'i disagree: they hold that death never extinguishes a debt, so suretyship for the deceased remains valid. We will unpack these positions and their proofs to understand the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence).

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi rule for debts after death
Debts like sale price, dower, or loans are liabilities on the dhimma. They do not persist by mere dhimma; they require either property left by the deceased or a surety (kafil) that the deceased arranged while alive.
If neither exists, the debt is 'dropped' in this world, but afterlife accountability remains.
2
Identify the dispute on suretyship
Abu Hanifa says suretyship for the deceased's debt is invalid if no property/surety exists, because the debt is dropped here. The Sahibayn and al-Shafi'i say it remains valid because death does not free one from the debt.
This is a classic ikhtilaf based on different conceptions of the dhimma.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi proof is rational: The purpose of debt in this world is collection; if demand is impossible (deceased is dead), the debt becomes meaningless. Suretyship depends on valid demand; no demand → no suretyship.
Voluntary payment (tabarru') is allowed by consensus, showing the debt exists in the afterlife.
4
Analyze the opposing proof
The Sahibayn and Shafi'i argue that death does not extinguish the debt. The dhimma remains, so suretyship is valid as a voluntary transfer of liability. They cite the permissibility of voluntary payment as evidence that the debt still 'exists'.
They consider the worldly and afterlife aspects as intertwined; the debt is one reality.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Under Abu Hanifa, a person cannot become a guarantor for the debt of a deceased without property; under the other view, one can. This affects inheritance and family liability.
The text indicates the relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's.

وہ تمام احکام ساقط ہو جاتے ہیں جن کا زندگی میں بندہ مکلف ہوتا ہے، کیونکہ موت سے مکلف ہونے کی غرض ہی فوت ہو جاتی ہے، اور وہ غرض ہے اپنے اختیار سے ادا کرنا ، اور میت اختیار ندا در ہے، لہذا نماز، روزہ، زکوۃ وغیرہ دنیوی اعتبار سے اس پر ساقط شمار ہوں گے، لہذا اس کے ترکہ سے زکوۃ کی ادائیگی واجب نہ ہوگی ، البتہ اخروی اعتبار سے اس پر گناہ باقی رہے گا۔ جاننا چاہیے کہ احکام دو طرح کے ہیں (۱) احکام دنیوی (۲) احکام اخروی ، پھر احکام دنیوی چار قسم کے ہیں (۱) وہ احکام جو تکلیف کے قبیل سے ہیں، اس کا حکم اوپر بیان ہو گیا کہ تکلیف کے قبیل کے سب احکام میت سے ساقط ہو جاتے ہیں۔
وما شرع عليه لحاجة غيره : - احکام دنیوی کی دوسری قسم کو بیان کر رہے ہیں ، کہ وہ احکام جو میت پر دوسروں کے لیے مشروع ہوتے ہیں، ان کی تین قسمیں ہیں (۱) پہلی قسم یہ ہے کہ وہ میت پر ایسا حق ہو جو عین کے ساتھ متعلق ہو، تو وہ حق مین کے باقی رہنے کی وجہ سے موت کے بعد بھی باقی رہے گا جیسے شئی مرہون ، یا مبیع جو مشتری کو سپرد نہ کی ہو، یاور بیعت وغیرہ یعنی اگر میت کے پاس کسی نے کوئی چیز رہن رکھی ہے یا میت نے کوئی چیز بیچی ہے اور ابھی مبیع مشتری کو سپر دنہیں کی ہے، یا میت کے پاس کوئی چیز غصب کی ہوئی ہے، یاکسی کی امانت ہے تو ان تمام چیزوں میں صاحب حق کا حق عین شی سے متعلق رہے گا، لہذا یہ چیزیں میت کے ترکہ یا وصیت میں شامل نہ ہوں گی، دلیل یہ ہے کہ حقوق العباد میں عین شی مقصود ہوتی ہے فعل مقصود نہیں ہوتا، تو جب تک شئی موجود ہے، حق باقی رہے گا بر خلاف حقوق اللہ کے کہ ان میں فعل مقصود ہوتا ہے، یعنی اللہ بندہ کا فعل دیکھتا ہے کہ بندہ اپنے اختیار سے ادا کرتا ہے یا نہیں۔ (۲) دوسری قسم یہ ہے کہ وہ حق جو دوسرے کی ضرورت کی وجہ سے مشروع ہے، اگر وہ دین وقرض کے قبیل سے ہے جو واجب فی الذمہ ہوتا ہے جیسے دیون واجبه مثلا من بیچ ، یا مہر یا قرض وغیرہ تو یہ حق محض میت کے ذمہ کی وجہ سے باقی نہ رہے گا، بلکہ میت کے ذمہ کے ساتھ مال ملا ہو یعنی میت نے مال چھوڑا ہو، یا وہ چیز ملی ہو جس سے ذمہ مضبوط ہوتا ہے یعنی میت نے اپنا کفیل چھوڑا ہو، تو ان صورتوں میں دین باقی رہے گا، پہلی صورت میں مال سے دین وصول کیا جائے گا، اور دوسری صورت میں کفیل سے دین کا مطالبہ کیا جائے گا، اور اگر نہ مال چھوڑا ہونہ کفیل ہوتو دینوی اعتبار سے دین ساقط ہو جائے گا، لہذامیت کی اولاد سے دین کا مطالبہ نہیں کیا جائے گا، ہاں اخروی اعتبار سے میت پر مؤاخذہ ہوگا۔
چونکہ محض میت کے ذمہ کی وجہ سے دین باقی نہیں رہتا ہے، اس لئے امام ابو حنیفہ علیہ فرماتے ہیں کہ میت نے اگر مال نہیں چھوڑا اور نہ کفیل چھوڑا تو میت کی طرف سے دین کا کفیل بننا صحیح نہیں ہے، کیونکہ دینوی اعتبار سے اس کا دین ساقط ہو جاتا ہے، البتہ بطور احسان کے کوئی اس کی طرف سے قرض ادا کر دے تو بالا تفاق صحیح ہے، صاحبین اور امام شافعی فرماتے ہیں کہ میت کی طرف سے دین کا کفیل بنا صحیح ہے، چاہے اس نے مال و کفیل نہ چھوڑا ہو، کیونکہ موت انسان کو قرض سے بری نہیں کرتی ، اس لئے بطور تبرع کے قرض کی ادائیگی میت کی طرف سے درست ہے۔ بخلاف العبد المحجور: ایک اعتراض کا جواب ہے، جس کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ ذمہ کا ضعیف ہونا میت اور عبد مجور دونوں میں برابر ہے، تو پھر میت کی طرف سے کفالت صحیح نہیں ، اور عبد مجور کی طرف سے کفیل بنا صحیح ہے ایسا کیوں؟
اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ عبد مجور اگر کسی کے دین کا اقرار کرے تو اس کی طرف سے کفیل ہونا صحیح ہے، اس لئے کہ عبد مجور کے عاقل بالغ اور مکلف ہونے کی وجہ سے اس کا ذمہ کامل ہے، اور اگر آقا دین کی تصدیق کرے یا اس کو آزاد کرے تو فی الحال مطالبہ بھی صحیح ہے تو کفالت بھی صحیح ہے، کیوں کہ کفالت کا مدار مطالبہ کی صحت پر ہے یعنی جس سے مطالبہ صحیح ہے اس کی کفالت صحیح ہے۔ اور میت میں چونکہ مطالبہ درست نہیں تو کفالت بھی درست نہیں ہے ، اور اگر مولی دین کی تصدیق نہ کرے یا آزاد نہ کرے تو عبد مجور سے فی الحال اگرچہ مطالبہ درست نہیں ہے ، مگر اس سے مطالبہ ممکن ہے ، جب آقا آزاد کرے گا تو مطالبہ درست ہوگا، لہذا کفالت صحیح ہے۔
.
وانما ضمت اليه المالية :- ایک سوال مقدر کا جواب ہے، کہ جب عبد مجور کا ذمہ کامل ہے تو آقا کے حق میں اس کے ساتھ مالیت رقبہ کو کیوں ملایا گیا ہے، یعنی آقا کے حق میں اس کو مالیت کیوں مانا گیا، کہ یہ آقا کا مال ہے۔ اس کا جواب دے رہے ہیں کہ عبد مجور کا ذمہ خود اس کے حق میں تو کامل ہے کیوں کہ وہ عاقل بالغ ہے ، مگر مولی کے حق میں اس کا ذمہ ناقص ہے ، اور مولی کے حق میں ناقص اس لئے مانا گیا تا کہ مولی نقصان سے بچ جائے ، کیوں کہ اگر مولی کے حق اس کا ذمہ کامل مانا گیا تو غلام سے فی الحال مطالبہ درست ہوگا تو غلام آزاد ہو جائے گا ، اور اس میں مولی کا نقصان ہے، مولی کو نقصان سے بچانے کے لئے مولی کے حق میں ذمہ کے ساتھ مالیت رقبہ کو ملایا گیا، کہ یہ مولی کا رقبہ ہے، لہذا فی الحال غلام سے مطالبہ درست نہ رہا، اور نہ مولی کی مرضی کے بغیر اس کو فروخت کرنا درست رہا۔ (۳) تیسری قسم وہ احکام جو میت پر بطور صلہ واحسان کے مشروع ہیں جیسے محارم کا نفقہ، صدقۂ فطر وغیرہ تو یہ احکام بھی موت سے باطل ہو جائیں گے ، لہذا محارم کا نفقہ میت کے ذمہ گذشتہ ایام کا ہو، یا آئندہ سب ساقط ہو جائے گا، محارم کو یہ حق نہیں ہے کہ میراث سے وصول کریں۔ البتہ اگر میت نے صلہ وتبرع کی وصیت کی ہو تو تہائی مال سے اس کی وصیت نافذ کر دی جائے گی۔
All rulings for which a living person is obligated are dropped, because death removes the purpose of being obligated, which is performing with choice, and the deceased has no choice. Therefore, prayer, fasting, zakah, etc., are considered dropped from a worldly perspective, so paying zakah from his estate is not obligatory; however, from an afterlife perspective, sin remains. Know that rulings are of two types: (1) worldly rulings, (2) afterlife rulings. Then worldly rulings are four types: (1) rulings that are of the category of obligation; their ruling was stated above: all obligations of this category are dropped from the deceased. (2) Those legislated for the need of others - this explains the second type of worldly rulings: rulings legislated for the sake of others upon the deceased. They are three kinds: (1) A right that is attached to a specific thing, such that that right remains after death due to the continued existence of the thing, like a mortgaged item, or a sold item not yet delivered to the buyer, or a usurped item, or a trust. In all these, the right-holder's right is attached to the specific thing, so these items are not included in the deceased's estate or will. The proof is that in rights of people, the specific thing is intended, not the action. So as long as the thing exists, the right continues, contrary to rights of Allah where the action is intended. (2) [Second kind:] A right legislated due to another's need; if it is of the type of debt that is obligatory upon the dhimma, like compulsory debts, e.g., sale price, dower, loan, etc., then this right does not remain merely due to the deceased's dhimma; rather, if property is left by the deceased or something strengthening the dhimma, i.e., a surety, then the debt remains. In the first case, the debt is collected from the property; in the second, demand is made from the surety. If neither property nor surety is left, then from a worldly perspective the debt is dropped, so the debt cannot be demanded from the deceased's children, though from an afterlife perspective the deceased will be held accountable. Since mere dhimma of the deceased does not keep the debt alive, Imam Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) says: if the deceased left no property or surety, then becoming surety for the deceased's debt is not valid, because from a worldly perspective his debt is dropped. However, if someone voluntarily pays the debt on his behalf, it is valid by consensus. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and Imam al-Shafi'i say: becoming surety for the deceased's debt is valid even if he left no property or surety, because death does not free one from debt; therefore, voluntary payment of the debt on behalf of the deceased is valid. Unlike the restricted slave (abd mahjur): this answers an objection, namely that weak dhimma is common to the deceased and the restricted slave, so why is suretyship for the deceased invalid, but for the restricted slave valid? The answer: if the restricted slave acknowledges a debt, becoming surety for him is valid because he is sane, adult, and obligated, so his dhimma is complete. If the master confirms the debt or frees him, demand becomes valid immediately, so suretyship is valid, because suretyship depends on the validity of demand: whoever can be validly demanded, suretyship for him is valid. Since demand is not valid for the deceased, suretyship is not valid. And if the master does not confirm or free him, demand is not immediately valid for the slave, but demand is possible; when the master frees, demand becomes valid, so suretyship is valid. (Objection answered:) "and why is financial worth attached to it?" - this answers a hypothetical question: when the restricted slave's dhimma is complete, why is financial worth of his person attached to it, i.e., why is he considered property of the master? The answer is that the slave's dhimma is complete regarding himself, but regarding the master it is deficient. It is considered deficient for the master to protect the master from loss, because if the dhimma were considered complete regarding the master, immediate demand from the slave would be valid, leading to the slave's freedom, harming the master. To prevent harm, the slave's financial worth as property is attached to the dhimma regarding the master. Therefore, immediate demand from the slave is not valid, nor is selling him without the master's consent. (3) The third kind: rulings legislated for the deceased as a reward and favor, like maintenance of relatives, sadaqat al-fitr, etc. These rulings become void by death, so maintenance for past or future days is dropped; relatives have no right to collect it from the inheritance. However, if the deceased made a bequest as a favor or gift, it will be executed from one-third of the estate.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Husami discusses a fundamental usul principle: how death affects different types of legal obligations (ahkam). The author begins by stating a general rule: obligations that require personal choice (taklif) are dropped by death, because the very purpose of obligation is voluntary performance. Thus, acts of worship like prayer, fasting, and zakah are not due from the deceased's estate in this world, though the deceased may bear sin in the afterlife for neglecting them. The passage then classifies worldly rulings into four categories, focusing on the second category: rulings legislated for the sake of others. These are further divided into three subtypes. 

The first subtype is rights attached to a specific thing (haqq al-ayn). These survive death because the right is tied to the object itself, not to the deceased's action. Examples include collateral, sold goods not yet delivered, usurped property, and trusts. These items are not part of the estate; the owner reclaims them.

The second subtype is debts (dayn) like sale price, dower, or loans. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, such debts do not persist merely due to the deceased's dhimma (legal liability); they require either assets left behind or a surety (kafil) provided by the deceased. If neither exists, the debt is dropped in this world (though the deceased remains answerable to Allah). The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and Imam al-Shafi'i disagree: they hold that death does not extinguish debts, so suretyship for the deceased is valid even without assets. The author explains the Hanafi position by noting that worldly demand is impossible against a corpse, so suretyship (which depends on valid demand) is invalid. However, voluntary payment (tabarru') is allowed by consensus.

The text then addresses a counterexample: why can a restricted slave (abd mahjur) have a valid surety if his dhimma is weak like that of a deceased? The slave, being sane and adult, has a complete dhimma in himself; his restriction is only regarding transactions without the master's permission. If he acknowledges a debt, demand against him is possible (e.g., if the master confirms or frees him), so suretyship is valid. For the deceased, demand is impossible in this world, so suretyship is invalid. Additionally, the slave's financial worth as property is attached to his dhimma regarding the master's rights, to prevent the master's loss (e.g., the slave being sold for debt and becoming free).

Finally, the third subtype includes obligations of goodwill like maintenance of relatives and sadaqat al-fitr. These are voided by death because they are not debts but rewards; no claim can be made from the inheritance unless the deceased made a bequest (within one-third of the estate).

Key Terms
دین (dayn): A debt obligation that is a liability in the dhimma; it requires property or surety to persist after death in worldly terms.
کفالت (kafalah): Suretyship; a contract where a person guarantees the debt of another, making himself liable for demand.
مطالبہ (matalabah): Legal demand; the act of calling upon a debtor to pay. Suretyship depends on the possibility of valid demand.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, what is the key factor that determines whether a debt survives after death?
A) The presence of a written will.
B) The existence of property or a surety left by the deceased.
C) The number of heirs.
D) The relationship of the creditor to the deceased.

Key Takeaway
A deceased's debt persists in this world only if property or surety is left; without them, suretyship for that debt is invalid according to Imam Abu Hanifa.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

The Restricted Slave Analogy: Why Suretyship Valid for a Slave but Not a Deceased
Answering an objection using the restricted slave analogy to defend the Hanafi position on suretyship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi distinction between the deceased and the restricted slave (abd mahjur) regarding the validity of suretyship, and apply the underlying usul principle that suretyship depends on the possibility of valid demand.

Core Idea
A dead man can't be pursued; a living slave can eventually be – that's why suretyship works for the slave but not the corpse.

This lesson examines a sharp objection raised against Abu Hanifa's position on suretyship for the deceased: if the deceased's dhimma is weak, why can a person become surety for a restricted slave (abd mahjur), who also has a weak dhimma? The commentary resolves this by showing that the slave's dhimma is only weak relative to his master, not in itself. Because the slave is alive, sane, and adult, his dhimma is complete; demand against him is possible (if the master permits or frees him). For the deceased, demand is utterly impossible in this world. Thus, the analogy fails, and suretyship for a deceased remains invalid.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objection claims that since both the deceased and the restricted slave have a 'weak' dhimma, suretyship should be either valid or invalid for both. But Abu Hanifa holds it is invalid for the deceased and valid for the slave. Why?
This is a classic counter-analogy (qiyas ma'al-fariq) that the text must answer.
2
Examine the Hanafi response
The response explains that the slave's dhimma is complete in itself because he is a sane adult. The weakness is only toward his master (to protect the master's property). Demand against the slave is possible, either now (if master confirms) or in the future (if master frees him).
Hence, suretyship is valid because there is a potential for valid demand.
3
Contrast with the deceased
For the deceased, there is no possibility of demand in this world at all. He cannot acknowledge or be sued. Therefore, suretyship, which hinges on valid demand, is invalid.
The key distinction: possibility of demand vs. absolute impossibility.
4
Understand the 'financial worth' attachment
The slave's dhimma is supplemented by his value as property (maliyyah) regarding the master's rights, to prevent the master's loss. This does not affect suretyship for others; it only restricts immediate demand from the master's side.
This explains why the slave is considered 'deficient' only from the master's viewpoint.
5
Apply the principle
The underlying usul principle: 'al-kafalah tadurru 'ala sihhat al-matalabah' (suretyship revolves around the validity of demand). This principle explains the different rulings for the deceased and the slave.
This is a valuable qa'ida for understanding other suretyship issues.

وہ تمام احکام ساقط ہو جاتے ہیں جن کا زندگی میں بندہ مکلف ہوتا ہے، کیونکہ موت سے مکلف ہونے کی غرض ہی فوت ہو جاتی ہے، اور وہ غرض ہے اپنے اختیار سے ادا کرنا ، اور میت اختیار ندا در ہے، لہذا نماز، روزہ، زکوۃ وغیرہ دنیوی اعتبار سے اس پر ساقط شمار ہوں گے، لہذا اس کے ترکہ سے زکوۃ کی ادائیگی واجب نہ ہوگی ، البتہ اخروی اعتبار سے اس پر گناہ باقی رہے گا۔ جاننا چاہیے کہ احکام دو طرح کے ہیں (۱) احکام دنیوی (۲) احکام اخروی ، پھر احکام دنیوی چار قسم کے ہیں (۱) وہ احکام جو تکلیف کے قبیل سے ہیں، اس کا حکم اوپر بیان ہو گیا کہ تکلیف کے قبیل کے سب احکام میت سے ساقط ہو جاتے ہیں۔
وما شرع عليه لحاجة غيره : - احکام دنیوی کی دوسری قسم کو بیان کر رہے ہیں ، کہ وہ احکام جو میت پر دوسروں کے لیے مشروع ہوتے ہیں، ان کی تین قسمیں ہیں (۱) پہلی قسم یہ ہے کہ وہ میت پر ایسا حق ہو جو عین کے ساتھ متعلق ہو، تو وہ حق مین کے باقی رہنے کی وجہ سے موت کے بعد بھی باقی رہے گا جیسے شئی مرہون ، یا مبیع جو مشتری کو سپرد نہ کی ہو، یاور بیعت وغیرہ یعنی اگر میت کے پاس کسی نے کوئی چیز رہن رکھی ہے یا میت نے کوئی چیز بیچی ہے اور ابھی مبیع مشتری کو سپر دنہیں کی ہے، یا میت کے پاس کوئی چیز غصب کی ہوئی ہے، یاکسی کی امانت ہے تو ان تمام چیزوں میں صاحب حق کا حق عین شی سے متعلق رہے گا، لہذا یہ چیزیں میت کے ترکہ یا وصیت میں شامل نہ ہوں گی، دلیل یہ ہے کہ حقوق العباد میں عین شی مقصود ہوتی ہے فعل مقصود نہیں ہوتا، تو جب تک شئی موجود ہے، حق باقی رہے گا بر خلاف حقوق اللہ کے کہ ان میں فعل مقصود ہوتا ہے، یعنی اللہ بندہ کا فعل دیکھتا ہے کہ بندہ اپنے اختیار سے ادا کرتا ہے یا نہیں۔ (۲) دوسری قسم یہ ہے کہ وہ حق جو دوسرے کی ضرورت کی وجہ سے مشروع ہے، اگر وہ دین وقرض کے قبیل سے ہے جو واجب فی الذمہ ہوتا ہے جیسے دیون واجبه مثلا من بیچ ، یا مہر یا قرض وغیرہ تو یہ حق محض میت کے ذمہ کی وجہ سے باقی نہ رہے گا، بلکہ میت کے ذمہ کے ساتھ مال ملا ہو یعنی میت نے مال چھوڑا ہو، یا وہ چیز ملی ہو جس سے ذمہ مضبوط ہوتا ہے یعنی میت نے اپنا کفیل چھوڑا ہو، تو ان صورتوں میں دین باقی رہے گا، پہلی صورت میں مال سے دین وصول کیا جائے گا، اور دوسری صورت میں کفیل سے دین کا مطالبہ کیا جائے گا، اور اگر نہ مال چھوڑا ہونہ کفیل ہوتو دینوی اعتبار سے دین ساقط ہو جائے گا، لہذامیت کی اولاد سے دین کا مطالبہ نہیں کیا جائے گا، ہاں اخروی اعتبار سے میت پر مؤاخذہ ہوگا۔
چونکہ محض میت کے ذمہ کی وجہ سے دین باقی نہیں رہتا ہے، اس لئے امام ابو حنیفہ علیہ فرماتے ہیں کہ میت نے اگر مال نہیں چھوڑا اور نہ کفیل چھوڑا تو میت کی طرف سے دین کا کفیل بننا صحیح نہیں ہے، کیونکہ دینوی اعتبار سے اس کا دین ساقط ہو جاتا ہے، البتہ بطور احسان کے کوئی اس کی طرف سے قرض ادا کر دے تو بالا تفاق صحیح ہے، صاحبین اور امام شافعی فرماتے ہیں کہ میت کی طرف سے دین کا کفیل بنا صحیح ہے، چاہے اس نے مال و کفیل نہ چھوڑا ہو، کیونکہ موت انسان کو قرض سے بری نہیں کرتی ، اس لئے بطور تبرع کے قرض کی ادائیگی میت کی طرف سے درست ہے۔ بخلاف العبد المحجور: ایک اعتراض کا جواب ہے، جس کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ ذمہ کا ضعیف ہونا میت اور عبد مجور دونوں میں برابر ہے، تو پھر میت کی طرف سے کفالت صحیح نہیں ، اور عبد مجور کی طرف سے کفیل بنا صحیح ہے ایسا کیوں؟
اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ عبد مجور اگر کسی کے دین کا اقرار کرے تو اس کی طرف سے کفیل ہونا صحیح ہے، اس لئے کہ عبد مجور کے عاقل بالغ اور مکلف ہونے کی وجہ سے اس کا ذمہ کامل ہے، اور اگر آقا دین کی تصدیق کرے یا اس کو آزاد کرے تو فی الحال مطالبہ بھی صحیح ہے تو کفالت بھی صحیح ہے، کیوں کہ کفالت کا مدار مطالبہ کی صحت پر ہے یعنی جس سے مطالبہ صحیح ہے اس کی کفالت صحیح ہے۔ اور میت میں چونکہ مطالبہ درست نہیں تو کفالت بھی درست نہیں ہے ، اور اگر مولی دین کی تصدیق نہ کرے یا آزاد نہ کرے تو عبد مجور سے فی الحال اگرچہ مطالبہ درست نہیں ہے ، مگر اس سے مطالبہ ممکن ہے ، جب آقا آزاد کرے گا تو مطالبہ درست ہوگا، لہذا کفالت صحیح ہے۔
.
وانما ضمت اليه المالية :- ایک سوال مقدر کا جواب ہے، کہ جب عبد مجور کا ذمہ کامل ہے تو آقا کے حق میں اس کے ساتھ مالیت رقبہ کو کیوں ملایا گیا ہے، یعنی آقا کے حق میں اس کو مالیت کیوں مانا گیا، کہ یہ آقا کا مال ہے۔ اس کا جواب دے رہے ہیں کہ عبد مجور کا ذمہ خود اس کے حق میں تو کامل ہے کیوں کہ وہ عاقل بالغ ہے ، مگر مولی کے حق میں اس کا ذمہ ناقص ہے ، اور مولی کے حق میں ناقص اس لئے مانا گیا تا کہ مولی نقصان سے بچ جائے ، کیوں کہ اگر مولی کے حق اس کا ذمہ کامل مانا گیا تو غلام سے فی الحال مطالبہ درست ہوگا تو غلام آزاد ہو جائے گا ، اور اس میں مولی کا نقصان ہے، مولی کو نقصان سے بچانے کے لئے مولی کے حق میں ذمہ کے ساتھ مالیت رقبہ کو ملایا گیا، کہ یہ مولی کا رقبہ ہے، لہذا فی الحال غلام سے مطالبہ درست نہ رہا، اور نہ مولی کی مرضی کے بغیر اس کو فروخت کرنا درست رہا۔ (۳) تیسری قسم وہ احکام جو میت پر بطور صلہ واحسان کے مشروع ہیں جیسے محارم کا نفقہ، صدقۂ فطر وغیرہ تو یہ احکام بھی موت سے باطل ہو جائیں گے ، لہذا محارم کا نفقہ میت کے ذمہ گذشتہ ایام کا ہو، یا آئندہ سب ساقط ہو جائے گا، محارم کو یہ حق نہیں ہے کہ میراث سے وصول کریں۔ البتہ اگر میت نے صلہ وتبرع کی وصیت کی ہو تو تہائی مال سے اس کی وصیت نافذ کر دی جائے گی۔
All rulings for which a living person is obligated are dropped, because death removes the purpose of being obligated, which is performing with choice, and the deceased has no choice. Therefore, prayer, fasting, zakah, etc., are considered dropped from a worldly perspective, so paying zakah from his estate is not obligatory; however, from an afterlife perspective, sin remains. Know that rulings are of two types: (1) worldly rulings, (2) afterlife rulings. Then worldly rulings are four types: (1) rulings that are of the category of obligation; their ruling was stated above: all obligations of this category are dropped from the deceased. (2) Those legislated for the need of others - this explains the second type of worldly rulings: rulings legislated for the sake of others upon the deceased. They are three kinds: (1) A right that is attached to a specific thing, such that that right remains after death due to the continued existence of the thing, like a mortgaged item, or a sold item not yet delivered to the buyer, or a usurped item, or a trust. In all these, the right-holder's right is attached to the specific thing, so these items are not included in the deceased's estate or will. The proof is that in rights of people, the specific thing is intended, not the action. So as long as the thing exists, the right continues, contrary to rights of Allah where the action is intended. (2) [Second kind:] A right legislated due to another's need; if it is of the type of debt that is obligatory upon the dhimma, like compulsory debts, e.g., sale price, dower, loan, etc., then this right does not remain merely due to the deceased's dhimma; rather, if property is left by the deceased or something strengthening the dhimma, i.e., a surety, then the debt remains. In the first case, the debt is collected from the property; in the second, demand is made from the surety. If neither property nor surety is left, then from a worldly perspective the debt is dropped, so the debt cannot be demanded from the deceased's children, though from an afterlife perspective the deceased will be held accountable. Since mere dhimma of the deceased does not keep the debt alive, Imam Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) says: if the deceased left no property or surety, then becoming surety for the deceased's debt is not valid, because from a worldly perspective his debt is dropped. However, if someone voluntarily pays the debt on his behalf, it is valid by consensus. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and Imam al-Shafi'i say: becoming surety for the deceased's debt is valid even if he left no property or surety, because death does not free one from debt; therefore, voluntary payment of the debt on behalf of the deceased is valid. Unlike the restricted slave (abd mahjur): this answers an objection, namely that weak dhimma is common to the deceased and the restricted slave, so why is suretyship for the deceased invalid, but for the restricted slave valid? The answer: if the restricted slave acknowledges a debt, becoming surety for him is valid because he is sane, adult, and obligated, so his dhimma is complete. If the master confirms the debt or frees him, demand becomes valid immediately, so suretyship is valid, because suretyship depends on the validity of demand: whoever can be validly demanded, suretyship for him is valid. Since demand is not valid for the deceased, suretyship is not valid. And if the master does not confirm or free him, demand is not immediately valid for the slave, but demand is possible; when the master frees, demand becomes valid, so suretyship is valid. (Objection answered:) "and why is financial worth attached to it?" - this answers a hypothetical question: when the restricted slave's dhimma is complete, why is financial worth of his person attached to it, i.e., why is he considered property of the master? The answer is that the slave's dhimma is complete regarding himself, but regarding the master it is deficient. It is considered deficient for the master to protect the master from loss, because if the dhimma were considered complete regarding the master, immediate demand from the slave would be valid, leading to the slave's freedom, harming the master. To prevent harm, the slave's financial worth as property is attached to the dhimma regarding the master. Therefore, immediate demand from the slave is not valid, nor is selling him without the master's consent. (3) The third kind: rulings legislated for the deceased as a reward and favor, like maintenance of relatives, sadaqat al-fitr, etc. These rulings become void by death, so maintenance for past or future days is dropped; relatives have no right to collect it from the inheritance. However, if the deceased made a bequest as a favor or gift, it will be executed from one-third of the estate.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Husami discusses a fundamental usul principle: how death affects different types of legal obligations (ahkam). The author begins by stating a general rule: obligations that require personal choice (taklif) are dropped by death, because the very purpose of obligation is voluntary performance. Thus, acts of worship like prayer, fasting, and zakah are not due from the deceased's estate in this world, though the deceased may bear sin in the afterlife for neglecting them. The passage then classifies worldly rulings into four categories, focusing on the second category: rulings legislated for the sake of others. These are further divided into three subtypes. 

The first subtype is rights attached to a specific thing (haqq al-ayn). These survive death because the right is tied to the object itself, not to the deceased's action. Examples include collateral, sold goods not yet delivered, usurped property, and trusts. These items are not part of the estate; the owner reclaims them.

The second subtype is debts (dayn) like sale price, dower, or loans. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, such debts do not persist merely due to the deceased's dhimma (legal liability); they require either assets left behind or a surety (kafil) provided by the deceased. If neither exists, the debt is dropped in this world (though the deceased remains answerable to Allah). The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and Imam al-Shafi'i disagree: they hold that death does not extinguish debts, so suretyship for the deceased is valid even without assets. The author explains the Hanafi position by noting that worldly demand is impossible against a corpse, so suretyship (which depends on valid demand) is invalid. However, voluntary payment (tabarru') is allowed by consensus.

The text then addresses a counterexample: why can a restricted slave (abd mahjur) have a valid surety if his dhimma is weak like that of a deceased? The slave, being sane and adult, has a complete dhimma in himself; his restriction is only regarding transactions without the master's permission. If he acknowledges a debt, demand against him is possible (e.g., if the master confirms or frees him), so suretyship is valid. For the deceased, demand is impossible in this world, so suretyship is invalid. Additionally, the slave's financial worth as property is attached to his dhimma regarding the master's rights, to prevent the master's loss (e.g., the slave being sold for debt and becoming free).

Finally, the third subtype includes obligations of goodwill like maintenance of relatives and sadaqat al-fitr. These are voided by death because they are not debts but rewards; no claim can be made from the inheritance unless the deceased made a bequest (within one-third of the estate).

Key Terms
عبد محجور (abd mahjur): A slave who is restricted from transacting without the master's permission; his dhimma is complete regarding himself but deficient regarding the master.
ذمہ (dhimma): Legal personality or capacity to incur obligations; it is the basis for liability.
مطالبہ (matalabah): Legal demand; the act of calling upon a debtor to pay. Suretyship depends on the possibility of valid demand.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why is suretyship valid for a restricted slave but not for a deceased?
A) Because the slave's master can always pay the debt.
B) Because demand against the slave is possible (now or in the future), whereas demand against the deceased is impossible.
C) Because the deceased has no dhimma at all.
D) Because suretyship is only valid for living persons with property.

Key Takeaway
Suretyship for a restricted slave is valid because demand is possible; for a deceased it is invalid because demand is impossible, despite both having weak dhimmah in some respects.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
8 min
The Effect of Death on Rulings: Void, Surviving, and the Criterion of Dhimma
Classification of rulings after death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that the validity of a ruling after death depends on the continued possibility of claim (mutalaba) and the nature of the dhimma, using the example of kafala.

Core Idea
Death nullifies rulings requiring claim (mutalaba) but preserves rulings based on need because dhimma remains for need.

This passage from Al-Fayd al-Hijazi explores a foundational usul question: when a person dies, which of their legal obligations survive and which become void? The author presents a three-part classification and then delves into a key dispute about guarantees (kafala). Through the analogy of a restricted slave, we learn that the decisive factor is not simply the weakness of dhimma, but the possibility of future claim. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and shows how it governs rulings like kitaba and the washing of a spouse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of rulings after death
From the Urdu and Arabic parts, extract the three categories: (1) rulings that become void (e.g., debts, kafala, maintenance obligations, sadaqat al-fitr); (2) rulings that survive for the deceased's need (e.g., shroud, debt payment, bequests, inheritance, kitaba, husband's right to be washed by wife); (3) rulings of the afterlife (continue as if alive).
The third category is eschatological and not subject to worldly enforcement.
2
Understand the ikhtilaf on kafala
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that kafala (guarantee) for the deceased's debt is invalid because the deceased's dhimma is completely null for worldly claims. The Sahibayn and Imam al-Shafi'i argue it is valid because death does not exempt from liability; payment as a donation is valid.
The text sides with Abu Hanifa.
3
Analyze the Hanafi reasoning using the restricted slave analogy
The apparent contradiction is resolved by examining the possibility of claim (mutalaba). The restricted slave has a complete dhimma in himself (he is mukallaf), so claim is possible when the master confirms or frees him. The deceased has no future possibility of claim, so kafala is invalid.
This is a qiyas khafi (hidden analogy) distinguishing the two cases based on the illa of possibility of claim.
4
Apply the principle to other rulings (kitaba, washing husband)
The text gives examples: kitaba remains valid after death because the slave's need for freedom continues; a woman may wash her deceased husband because his ownership over her for 'idda needs persists. These are rulings that survive because they fulfill the deceased's need.
Contrast with the wife's death: her capacity as owned ends, so the husband cannot wash her.
5
Distinguish rulings of the afterlife
The passage concludes that in matters of the afterlife (e.g., reward and punishment for deeds), the deceased is treated as alive. This is a separate category not based on worldly claim or need.
This serves as a reminder of the ultimate purpose of legal rulings.

الفیضُ الحجازي
شرح اردو المنتخب الى امى
دین ساقط ہو جاتا ہے، البتہ بطور احسان کے کوئی اس کی طرف سے قرض ادا کر دے تو بالا تفاق صحیح ہے، صاحبین اور امام شافعی فرماتے ہیں کہ میت کی طرف سے دین کا کفیل بنا صحیح ہے، چاہے اس نے مال و کفیل نہ چھوڑا ہو، کیونکہ موت
انسان کو قرض سے بری نہیں کرتی ، اس لئے بطور تبرع کے قرض کی ادائیگی میت کی طرف سے درست ہے۔ بخلاف العبد المحجور: ایک اعتراض کا جواب ہے، جس کا حاصل یہ ہے کہ ذمہ کا ضعیف ہونا میت اور عبد مجور دونوں میں برابر ہے، تو پھر میت کی طرف سے کفالت صحیح نہیں ، اور عبد مجور کی طرف سے کفیل بنا صحیح ہے ایسا کیوں؟
اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ عبد مجور اگر کسی کے دین کا اقرار کرے تو اس کی طرف سے کفیل ہونا صحیح ہے، اس لئے کہ عبد مجور کے عاقل بالغ اور مکلف ہونے کی وجہ سے اس کا ذمہ کامل ہے، اور اگر آقا دین کی تصدیق کرے یا اس کو آزاد کرے تو فی الحال مطالبہ بھی صحیح ہے تو کفالت بھی صحیح ہے، کیوں کہ کفالت کا مدار مطالبہ کی صحت پر ہے یعنی جس سے مطالبہ صحیح ہے اس کی کفالت صحیح ہے۔ اور میت میں چونکہ مطالبہ درست نہیں تو کفالت بھی درست نہیں ہے ، اور اگر مولی دین کی تصدیق نہ کرے یا آزاد نہ کرے تو عبد مجور سے فی الحال اگرچہ مطالبہ درست نہیں ہے ، مگر اس سے مطالبہ ممکن ہے ، جب آقا آزاد کرے گا تو مطالبہ درست ہوگا، لہذا کفالت صحیح ہے۔
.
وانما ضمت اليه المالية :- ایک سوال مقدر کا جواب ہے، کہ جب عبد مجور کا ذمہ کامل ہے تو آقا کے حق میں اس کے ساتھ مالیت رقبہ کو کیوں ملایا گیا ہے، یعنی آقا کے حق میں اس کو مالیت کیوں مانا گیا، کہ یہ آقا کا مال ہے۔ اس کا جواب دے رہے ہیں کہ عبد مجور کا ذمہ خود اس کے حق میں تو کامل ہے کیوں کہ وہ عاقل بالغ ہے ، مگر مولی کے حق میں اس کا ذمہ ناقص ہے ، اور مولی کے حق میں ناقص اس لئے مانا گیا تا کہ مولی نقصان سے بچ جائے ، کیوں کہ اگر مولی کے حق اس کا ذمہ کامل مانا گیا تو غلام سے فی الحال مطالبہ درست ہوگا تو غلام آزاد ہو جائے گا ، اور اس میں مولی کا نقصان ہے، مولی کو نقصان سے بچانے کے لئے مولی کے حق میں ذمہ کے ساتھ مالیت رقبہ کو ملایا گیا، کہ یہ مولی کا
رقبہ ہے، لہذا فی الحال غلام سے مطالبہ درست نہ رہا، اور نہ مولی کی مرضی کے بغیر اس کو فروخت کرنا درست رہا۔ (۳) تیسری قسم وہ احکام جو میت پر بطور صلہ واحسان کے مشروع ہیں جیسے محارم کا نفقہ، صدقۂ فطر وغیرہ تو یہ احکام بھی موت سے باطل ہو جائیں گے ، لہذا محارم کا نفقہ میت کے ذمہ گذشتہ ایام کا ہو، یا آئندہ سب ساقط ہو جائے
گا، محارم کو یہ حق نہیں ہے کہ میراث سے وصول کریں۔ البتہ اگر میت نے صلہ وتبرع کی وصیت کی ہو تو تہائی مال سے اس کی وصیت نافذ کر دی جائے گی۔

وَأَمَّا الَّذِي شُرِعَ لَهُ فَبِنَاءً عَلَى حَاجَتِهِ وَالْمَوْتُ لَا يَيْنَا فِي لَهُ مَا يَنْقَضِي بِهِ الْحَاجَةُ وَلِذْلِكَ قُدِمَ جَهَازَهُ ثُمَّ دُيُونُهُ ثُمَّ وَصَايَاهُ مِنْ ثُلُثِهِ ثُمَّ وَجَبَ الْمَوَارِيثُ بِطَرِيقِ الْخِلَافَةِ عَنْهُ نَظْرًا لَهُ وَلِهَذَا بَقِيَتِ الْكِتَابَةُ بَعْدَ مَوْتِ الْمَوْلٰى وَبَعْدَ مَوْتِ الْمُكَاتَب عَنْ وَفَاءٍ وَقُلْنَا اِنَ الْمَرْأَةَ تَغْسِلُ زَوجَهَا بَعْدَ الْمَوْتِ فِي عِدَتِهَالان الزَّوْجَ مَالِكٌ فَبَقِيَ مِلْكُهُ إِلى اِنْقِضَاءِ الْعِدَّةِ فِيْمَا هُوَ مِنْ حَوَائِجِهِ خَاصَّةً بِخِلَافِ مَا إِذَا مَاتَتِ الْمَرْأَةُ لِاَنَّهَا مَمْلُوكَةٌ وَقَدْ بَطَلَتْ اَهْلِيَّةُ المَمْلُوكِيةِ بالمَوْتِ وَلِهَذَا تَعَلَّقَ حَقُّ الْمَقْتُولِ بِالذِيَةِ إِذَا انْقَلَبَ الْقِصَاصُ مَالًا وَإِنْ كَانَ الْأَصْلُ وَهُوَ الْقِصَاصُ يَثْبُتُ لِلْوَرَثَهِ ابْتَدَاءً بِسَبَبٍ انْقَعَدَ لِلْمُوْرِثِ لِأَنَّهُ يَجِبْ عِنْدَ اِنْقِضَاءِ الْحَيَوةِ وَعِنْدَ ذَلِكَ لَا يَجِبْ لَهُ إِلَّا مَا يَضْطَرُ إِلَيْهِ لِحَاجَتِهِ فَفَارَقَ الْخَلْفُ الْاَصَلَ لِاِخْتِلَافَ حَالِهِمَا وَاَمَّا اَحْكَامُ الْآخِرَةِ فَلَهُ فِيهَا حُكْمُ الْأَحْيَاءِ لِاَنَّ الْقَبْرَ لِلْمَيِّتِ فِي حُكْمِ الْآخِرَةِ كَالرَّحِمِ لِلْمَاءِ وَ الْمَهْدِ لِلطِّفْلِ فِي حَقِّ الدُّنْيَا وُضِعَ فِيْهِ لِأَحْكَامِ الْآخِرَةِ رَوْضَةُ دَارٍ أَوْ حُفْرَةُ نَارٍ وَنَرْجُو اللَّهَ تَعَالَى أَنْ يُصَيْرَهُ لَنَا رَوْضَةً
بِكَرَمِهِ وَفَضْلِهِ
(Urdu) Debt becomes void. However, if someone pays it as a favor, it is valid by consensus. The Sahibayn and Imam al-Shafi'i say: It is valid to become a guarantor (kafil) for the debt of the deceased, even if he left no property or guarantor, because death does not exempt a person from debt. So payment of debt as a donation on behalf of the deceased is valid. Contrary to the restricted slave (abd mahjur): This is the answer to an objection. The summary is that the weakness of the dhimma is equal in both the deceased and the restricted slave, so why is kafala on behalf of the deceased not valid while on behalf of the restricted slave it is valid? The answer is that if the restricted slave acknowledges a debt, then becoming a guarantor for him is valid, because due to the slave being an adult of sound mind and legally responsible (mukallaf), his dhimma is complete. And if the master confirms the debt or frees him, immediate claim is valid, so kafala is valid, because kafala is based on the validity of claim. In the deceased, since claim is not valid, kafala is also not valid. But if the master does not confirm the debt or free him, immediate claim from the restricted slave is not valid, but claim is possible (in the future); when the master frees him, claim becomes valid, so kafala is valid. (The author's statement) 'And why is property (maliyya) attached to it' is the answer to a presumed question: when the restricted slave's dhimma is complete, why is property (value of his person) attached to it in relation to the master, i.e., why is it considered property of the master? The answer: the slave's dhimma is complete for himself, but towards the master it is deficient. It is considered deficient towards the master to prevent loss to the master, because if his dhimma were considered complete towards the master, immediate claim from the slave would be valid and the slave would be freed, causing loss to the master. To protect the master from loss, property (value of the slave) is attached to the dhimma towards the master, i.e., he is the master's property. Thus immediate claim from the slave is not valid, nor is selling him without the master's consent valid. (3) The third category: rulings prescribed for the deceased as a reward and favor (silat wa ihsan), such as maintenance of mahram relatives, sadaqat al-fitr, etc. — these rulings also become void by death. So maintenance of mahram relatives, whether for past days or future, all become void; the mahram relatives do not have the right to claim from the inheritance. However, if the deceased made a will (wasiyya) for reward and donation, his will shall be executed from one-third of his property. (Arabic) As for that which is prescribed for him (the deceased), it is based on his need, and death does not negate what fulfills his need. Therefore his shroud (jihaz) is given precedence, then his debts, then his bequests from one-third, then inheritance becomes obligatory by way of succession on his behalf, out of consideration for him. For this reason, kitaba (contract of manumission) remains valid after the death of the master and after the death of the mukatab (if he leaves sufficient property). And we said: a woman may wash her husband after his death during her 'idda, because the husband is a owner (malik), so his ownership remains until the end of the 'idda specifically in matters of his needs. Contrary to when the wife dies, because she is owned, and the capacity of being owned becomes void by death. And for this reason, the right of the murdered person attaches to blood-money (diya) when retaliation (qisas) is converted into property, even though the original (qisas) is established for the heirs initially due to a cause that arose for the testator. That is because (qisas) becomes obligatory at the end of life, and at that time, only what is necessary for his need is obligatory for him. So the successor differs from the original due to the difference in their conditions. As for the rulings of the afterlife, he (the deceased) has the status of the living in them, because the grave for the deceased in the context of the Hereafter is like the womb for the semen and the cradle for the child in worldly matters. He is placed in it for the rulings of the Hereafter: it is either a garden of paradise or a pit of hellfire. And we hope from Allah that He makes it a garden for us by His generosity and favor.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage is a legal classification from the Hanafi school regarding the effect of death on various rulings. The author divides rulings into three categories. First: rulings that become void with death, like debts (diyun) except if someone pays voluntarily, which is valid by consensus. There is a dispute: Imam Abu Hanifa holds that becoming a guarantor (kafala) for the deceased's debt is invalid because the deceased's dhimma is deficient and claim from him is impossible. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and al-Shafi'i argue it is valid because death does not exempt from liability; payment as a donation is valid. The author then addresses an objection: if both the deceased and a restricted slave have weak dhimma, why is kafala valid for the slave but not the deceased? The answer: the slave's dhimma is complete in itself (he is a mukallaf adult), so claim is possible when the master confirms or frees him. For the deceased, claim is permanently impossible. Also, the slave's property (his value) is attached to his dhimma towards the master to protect the master's interest, which explains the difference. Second: rulings prescribed for the deceased's own need, such as preparation of his corpse, payment of debts, execution of bequests up to one-third, and inheritance. These continue after death because they fulfill his need. Examples given: the kitaba contract remains valid after the death of either party (if the slave can pay), and a woman may wash her deceased husband during her 'idda because his ownership of her (as a wife) continues for her 'idda period for certain needs. Third: rulings prescribed as reward, like maintenance of mahram relatives and sadaqat al-fitr, become void by death. However, if he made a will for such charity, it is executed from one-third of his estate. The passage concludes with a reminder that in afterlife matters, the deceased retains the status of the living, and the grave becomes either a garden or a pit.

Key Terms
كَفَالَة (kafāla): Guarantee or surety; a contract where one person becomes responsible for another's debt or appearance.
عَبْد مَحْجُور (ʿabd maḥjūr): A slave who is restricted from dealing in property due to being owned; his dhimma is complete for himself but deficient towards his master.
ذِمَّة (dhimma): Legal capacity to incur obligations; it is complete for a free adult mukallaf, deficient for a deceased or a restricted slave towards the master.
كِتَابَة (kitāba): A contract of manumission by which a slave agrees to pay certain sums to the master and becomes free upon payment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Abu Hanifa distinguish between a deceased person and a restricted slave when ruling on kafala?
A) Because the deceased has no dhimma at all, while the slave has a complete dhimma.
B) Because the slave is capable of future claim (mutalaba), while the deceased is not.
C) Because the slave can be freed, but the deceased cannot.
D) Because the deceased left property, but the slave did not.

Key Takeaway
The survival of a ruling after death depends on the continued possibility of claim (mutalaba) and the nature of the deceased's need; kafala fails because claim is impossible.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling
intermediate
8 min
Rulings for the Deceased Based on Need (Hajah)
Explaining the third category of rulings for the deceased based on need

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that certain rulings persist after death based on the deceased's need (hajah) and apply this principle to examples such as kitabah, washing of spouse, and conversion of qisas.

Core Idea
Death does not erase need — rights that fulfill the deceased's hajah survive to the extent required.

In this passage, the author explains the third category of rulings for the deceased: those legislated for his own benefit based on his need (hajah). While death ends legal capacity for most transactions, the Hanafi school recognizes that certain rights and obligations continue because the deceased remains in need of them—burial, debt clearance, and even eschatological benefit. This lesson unpacks the principle and its application to several furu' cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the underlying principle
State that rulings for the deceased that are legislated for his own benefit are based on his need (hajah). Death does not negate this need, so the rulings remain to fulfill it.
The Arabic phrase 'فَبِنَاءً عَلَى حَاجَتِهِ' is explicit: 'based on his need.'
2
Examine the order of priorities from the estate
From the estate, first priority is shrouding (tajhiz), then debts, then bequests from one-third, then inheritance. Each fulfills a specific need of the deceased.
Inheritance is for the deceased's reward, as heirs benefit from his wealth and he earns reward.
3
Understand the continuation of kitabah contract
The kitabah (contract of manumission by installments) remains valid after the death of either the master or the slave (if wealth remains) because there is a need to complete freedom and gain reward.
This shows that need (hajah) overrides the general rule that contracts terminate at death.
4
Analyze the rule regarding spouse washing
A wife may wash her deceased husband during her 'iddah because the husband is an owner (malik) and his ownership of certain marital rights persists to fulfill his need. The converse does not apply because the wife is owned (mamluka) and her capacity ends at death.
Contrast this with the case of the wife's death: the husband cannot wash her because she ceases to be owned.
5
Grasp the conversion of qisas to diyah
When qisas (retribution) is converted to property (diyah), the right attaches to the deceased's estate even though the original right was for the heirs. This is because at the moment of death, only the deceased's need (e.g., compensation or deterrence) is considered.
The successor (heir) differs from the original (deceased) due to different states.
6
Connect to Hereafter: the grave as a realm of need
In rulings of the Hereafter, the deceased is considered alive for reward and punishment. The grave is likened to a womb or cradle, emphasizing that needs continue.
This reinforces that the deceased is not entirely cut off from legal and spiritual consequences.

Source Text
